About the Author
Denise Sutherland is an Australian syndicated puzzle author. She has a back-
ground in science and graphic design and is a professional member of the 
Australian Society of Authors. She is also an established artist and classically 
trained musician.
Denise has always been interested in mysteries and puzzles. As a child she 
was captivated by the Encyclopedia Brown books, which lead inevitably on to 
the works of Joan Aiken, Georges Simenon, Colin Dexter, Reginald Hill, P.D. 
James, and many others; murder mysteries are still her favorite genre, along 
with anything by Terry Pratchett.
There is no offi cial training to become a puzzle writer. It’s an unusual mix 
of skills: a fascination with words, numbers, puzzles, logic, and patterns; an 
ability to deconstruct and then reconstruct puzzles; and years of practice. 
Denise started writing puzzles for her younger sisters and brothers when she 
was young but got serious about teaching herself to write crosswords and 
other puzzles when her own children were little, in the late 1980s.
Denise has run her own graphic design business, Sutherland Studios, since 
1997; however, she now concentrates almost solely on puzzles. 
She has written puzzles for many groups, from home schooling groups, 
regional Slow Food organizations, and the Australian Science Archives 
Project to the Australian sugarcane industry and Capital BlueCross (USA). 
She has several syndicated puzzle series, including her unique Secret Shape 
word search puzzles. Her puzzles are published in a wide variety of newspa-
pers and magazines in Australia and around the world.
Denise has researched, written, and illustrated three themed puzzle books 
for several organizations (the Royal Flying Doctor Service of Australia, the 
Alice Springs School of the Air, and the Royal Society for the Prevention of 
Cruelty to Animals), and two books on the history and heritage of her home-
town, Canberra (the national capital of Australia).
Denise has lived in the States at various times in her life, in New Haven, 
Connecticut; Tucson, Arizona; and Boulder, Colorado, and currently lives in 
Canberra with her astrophysicist husband Dr. Ralph and their kids Rodger and 
Jenny. She is a completely obsessed knitter, and also enjoys BookCrossing, 
cryptic crosswords, Middle Eastern dancing, and watercolor painting.
You can visit her Web site for free puzzles and knitting patterns, and to see 
her paintings (sutherland-studios.com.au).

To be cut in half and shared equally:
For my husband Ralph, whose support, silliness, programming skills, and 
love I rely on utterly. Yours forever.

Introduction
H
umans are a funny bunch. As in funny ha ha. We have had an enduring 
love of fooling around with language probably since words existed. The 
ancient Babylonians wrote riddles, as did the Persians, Arabs, and Greeks. The 
Romans especially loved a good acrostic. Anagrams, puns, tongue twisters, 
and other wordy diversions have been around for hundreds if not thousands of 
years across all languages.
The last hundred years or so have seen a huge explosion in word games, 
including the notable invention of the crossword puzzle. Everyone has played 
I Spy, Hangman, or Scrabble and told truly dreadful puns (or is that just me?). 
There seems to be no end to the appetite for puzzle books (from pocket sized 
to jumbo collections), not to mention online puzzles. Many cellphones even 
have word games, so you don’t have to be without your puzzles for an instant!
Word searches are a significant part of this wordy world. Although not every-
one considers them “serious” word puzzles, they may be even more popular 
than crosswords.
About This Book
I’ve written this book in part to help raise the humble word search to its 
rightful place. I share a little history, explain how best to solve word searches 
of different types, and reveal some of my secrets on how to write your own.
But don’t forget the puzzles; they’re the main reason you picked up this book 
at all, and my favorite part of the book, too. This lovely volume contains more 
than 250 brand-new word search puzzles, from your run-of-the-mill word 
search to seven other variations. All the puzzles come in Easy, Medium, and 
Hard levels, and have been carefully handcrafted — no computer-generated 
puzzles here!
Whether you’re doing these puzzles to help stay mentally on the ball (more 
on that in Chapter 1), or just to chill out and relax on vacation, I’m sure you’ll 
enjoy what follows. Trust me; after all, I’m a puzzle writer — would I lie to you?

2
Word Searches For Dummies 
Conventions Used in This Book
I use a few conventions in this book to make it easy for you to navigate:
 
✓ I use italics to introduce new terms, and then I define those terms.
 
✓ The keywords for bulleted lists appear in bold text. Bold also denotes 
the list words in the Story Word Searches in Chapter 4.
 
✓ I used the New Oxford American Dictionary, 2nd Edition (Oxford 
University Press), as my standard reference in writing these puzzles.
 
✓ A couple of chapters give you some Web addresses to online resources. 
To make these URLs easier to spot and read, they’re printed in monofont.
 
 Also, during the layout of this book, some Web addresses may have 
needed to run across two lines of text. If that has happened, rest 
assured that I haven’t put in any extra characters (such as hyphens) to 
indicate the break. So, when using one of these Web addresses, just type 
in exactly what you see on the book page, pretending as though the line 
break doesn’t exist.
 
✓ The word list is the list of words appearing next to or underneath the 
puzzle. These are the words you need to find in the puzzle.
 
✓ The letter grid is the puzzle grid formed of letters. This feature is usually 
square, but it may be in a different shape on occasion.
 
✓ In the Hints section (Chapter 12), I refer to positions in the letter grid 
by using a coordinate system, like in the game Battleship. The first 
coordinate (the letter) is the column position (horizontal axis), and the 
second coordinate (the number) is the row position (vertical axis). So a 
hint such as “CHIHUAHUA starts at (J, 3)” indicates that the first letter in 
CHIHUAHUA is at the intersection of the tenth column and third row of 
the puzzle. I give you more detail about this subject in Chapter 12.
In addition, I use this difficulty rating system for the puzzles:
 
✓ Easy: Most of the Easy puzzles in this book are very straightforward, 
and have the smallest grids. The words in the letter grid don’t have a lot 
of overlapping letters or as many backward or diagonal words. I also use 
a simpler vocabulary for these puzzles so that the words are hopefully 
ones you’re familiar with.
 
✓ Medium: The Medium level puzzles are larger, have words that overlap 
each other more, and use more diagonal and backward words. The word 
lists contain more difficult words, which are generally harder to find.

3
 Introduction
 
✓ Hard: The Hard puzzles have the biggest grids and much more difficult 
words to look for: foreign vocabulary, unusual terms, place names — 
basically, words you may need to look up in a dictionary. The words 
appear in all directions, and of course these puzzles have more back-
ward, short, and overlapping words than the Easy and Medium puzzles. 
These Hard puzzles often have an extra twist to them, such as extra 
words in the word list or jumbled hidden words. I let you know when a 
puzzle contains one of these extra challenges.
What You’re Not to Read
You can skip all of Part I and just head right on over to whichever puzzles 
you want to have a go at first! The Part of Tens chapters in Part V are also 
additional information not essential to the puzzles.
When you come across the occasional sidebar, you can safely ignore it as 
well. These shaded boxes contain some interesting snippets of information 
from the world of word puzzles, but they’re not central to the main book.
Foolish Assumptions
Word search puzzles have such a wide appeal and are so accessible to every-
one that all I’m assuming is that you can read English — you don’t even need 
to have a wide general knowledge or know how to spell well to do most of the 
puzzles in this book.
I do hope that you have an interest in words and are keen to have some relax-
ing fun. Perseverance is the main quality you need to solve all the puzzles. 
And a sense of fun — none of this nonsense needs to be taken seriously!
I suppose you may have some ulterior motives, such as improving your 
vocabulary or staying mentally active, but I won’t let that get in the way of 
serving you up a whole heap of fun puzzles!
How This Book Is Organized
This is mainly a book of word search puzzles, not a vast amount of writing 
about them. That said, a couple of its sections do introduce and explain this 
puzzle genre and give you some fun resources such as letter frequency lists 
and even instructions on how to write your own word search!

4
Word Searches For Dummies 
Part I: Working with Word Searches
This part gives you a little background history of word searches and a quick 
rundown of the benefits of solving puzzles. It also describes each of the eight 
word search varieties I give you in the book, plus some anagram and word 
games.
Part II: Starting with Straightforward 
Puzzles
This part contains heaps of straightforward word search puzzles: Simple 
Word Searches, Story Word Searches, Hidden Words, and Secret Shapes. 
These varieties are the sort you’re most likely to be familiar with from news-
papers and magazines, so they shouldn’t make your brain hurt (too much).
Part III: Moving On to Complex Puzzles
The chapters in Part III contain a collection of devious word searches — 
Scrambled Words, Quiz and Secret List Word Searches, and Winder Words — 
and other anagram-based word puzzles. I think I can fairly say that these puzzles 
are likely to make your brain hurt. But in a good way.
Part IV: Hints and Answers
This part is the one to turn to if you need a little help along the way (or need to 
resort to the answers before you tear your hair out). Chapter 12 contains a hint 
for every single puzzle in the book; I encourage you to check out these hints 
before turning to the answers. Chapter 13 has the answers to all the puzzles, 
with full grids so you can see where each word is placed in every puzzle. Avoid 
this last chapter for as long as possible; I don’t advocate ripping the pages out 
of the book or taping them together, but you get the idea — be strong!
Part V: The Part of Tens
This part shows you useful letter pattern tips that I hope you find invaluable 
in your puzzling adventures, both in this book and further afield. It also gives 
you a rundown of the benefits of doing word searches, some of which may 
surprise you, and a set of tips on how to write your own word search with 
only some graph paper and a pencil.

5
 Introduction
Icons Used in This Book
I use a few icons throughout the book to emphasize important information. 
This is what they look like and what they mean:
 
This icon alerts you to a handy hint or tip that may help in solving the word 
search puzzles or other wordy games.
 
When you see this icon, take special note! This icon highlights information 
that’s especially good to remember when solving these puzzles.
 
These warnings point out common pitfalls you may encounter when solving 
word puzzles.
Where to Go from Here
You don’t have to read this book from start to finish like you would a novel. 
Although you can do it this way, of course, you can also dip in and out as the 
mood takes you. Each section stands alone; one chapter may be connected 
with another, but I always give you a cross-reference if that’s the case.
If you want to know a little more about word search puzzles in general or find 
some solving hints and descriptions of the puzzle varieties in this book, head 
over to Part I.
If puzzles are what you want — and make that now — hightail it to Part II to 
get right into the word searches. To start with a challenge, go straight to the 
complex puzzles in Part III.
And if want to read about the benefits of doing word search puzzles or write 
one of your own, check out the Part of Tens chapters in Part V.
All I ask is that you avoid Chapter 13 (you know, the answers) for as long as 
possible (whether that’s ten minutes or ten days). Give the puzzles a good try 
before resorting to this part of the book.

6
Word Searches For Dummies 

Part I
Working with 
Word Searches 

In this part . . .
I 
introduce you to the wonderful world of word search 
puzzles! I explain some of the history of word searches 
and reveal handy techniques for solving these puzzles, 
as well as give you the tools of the trade. I also show you 
how solving puzzles can benefit your brain without feeling 
like homework.
I explain in detail how to solve each of the varieties of 
puzzles found in this book so that you’re ready for what-
ever lies ahead. With this knowledge, you can become 
Mr. or Ms. Word Search Expert — no name change required.

Chapter 1
Hunting through the World 
of Word Searches 
In This Chapter
▶ Discovering everything you need to know about word searches
▶ Considering the benefits of solving puzzles
▶ Investigating the history of word searches
W
hether you call them Word Searches, Word Finds, Word Seeks, Word 
Sleuths, Find-a-Words, Wonderwords, Mystery Words, Seek-a-Words, 
Seek and Finds, or even Sopa de letras, these puzzles are hugely popular 
worldwide. They may even be more popular than crosswords, which is 
saying something!
In this chapter, I tell you a little about each type of word search puzzle in the 
book. I also delve into some of the benefits of word searches and round off 
the chapter with a fun history of these popular puzzles.
Word Searches at a Glance
Word searches in their simplest form provide a list of words that have been 
placed in a grid of seemingly random letters. Your task is to discover the 
words within the grid. The words can show up in any direction but always 
run in a straight line. Well, almost always.
In this book, I’ve written eight different types of word search puzzles, plus a 
bunch of bonus anagram puzzles. You may have come across some of these 
puzzle flavors before, but some may be new to you. (Chapter 2 gives you tips 
for solving word searches in general, and anagram-style puzzles. And Parts II 
and III have an entire chapter devoted to each puzzle type.)

10
Part I: Working with Word Searches 
The puzzle varieties are
 
✓ Simple Word Searches: These puzzles are the popular plain-vanilla 
variety — they have a list of words that are hidden in a grid of letters in 
straight horizontal, vertical, and diagonal lines. Head to Chapter 3 to try 
your hand at these puzzles!
 
✓ Story Word Searches: Read a short story and search for the bold words 
from the article in the grid. Otherwise, these searches are the same as 
Simple Word Searches. Chapter 4 contains the Story Word Searches.
 
✓ Hidden Words: Find all the listed words in the grid, and the leftover grid 
letters spell out a hidden message. Be careful to find all the extra letters 
to get the right answer! Some of these puzzles are in different shapes, a 
nice break from squares. Check out Chapter 5 for a plethora of Hidden 
Words puzzles.
 
✓ Secret Shapes: These word search puzzles have gridlines on them; after 
you find all the words from the word list, color in the leftover squares to 
reveal a picture. You can also color in the words, and leave the leftover 
letters blank — either method works. Chapter 6 lets you take a shot at 
some Secret Shapes.
 
✓ Scrambled Words: This puzzle may look like a Simple Word Search at 
first, until you realize that the word list makes no sense! The letters of 
each word are muddled up, so you need to unscramble each entry and 
then look for it in the grid. You can find these puzzles in Chapter 7.
 
✓ Quiz Word Searches: These puzzles are the closest thing you can get 
to a crossword in the world of word searches. Solve each quiz question 
and then find your answers in the grid. You can also work backward by 
finding potential words in the grid and then matching them to the word 
list. Chapter 8 features these searches.
 
✓ Secret List Word Searches: These puzzles are quite a challenge — you 
need to discover the words in the grid without a list to guide you. I do 
give you the theme, the number of words to find, and hints for most of 
the words. Try these puzzles in Chapter 9.
 
✓ Winder Words: In these puzzles, the words don’t go in straight lines but 
rather turn on right angles. Words don’t wrap around onto themselves, 
but different words do overlap. Winder Words appear in Chapter 10.
 
✓ Anagrams and other word games: This category includes anagrams, 
jumbled words, and other puzzles that require you to shuffle letters 
around to form words. Chapter 11 gives you some of these puzzles.
This list may seem like a lot of puzzles, but brains equally as warped as mine 
have created even more word search variations. And given how inventive 
humans are, I’m sure new variations on word search puzzles will appear in 
the future!

11
 Chapter 1: Hunting through the World of Word Searches
Using Word Searches to Keep 
Your Mind Sharp
In Chapter 15, I discuss the many good reasons to get hooked on word 
searches. Here’s a quick rundown of some of the top reasons:
 
✓ They improve your vocabulary and spelling.
 
✓ They’re very portable — you need only a pen or pencil.
 
✓ They’re fun! (And who doesn’t need more of that in life?)
 
✓ They’re easy enough for children, so they’re a great activity for families 
to do together.
Those benefits are pretty easy to understand. Less clear-cut is the link 
between doing puzzles like word searches and benefitting your long-term 
brain health.
Understanding dementia
The benefit of mental exercise (including solving puzzles) for your long-term 
mental health has been the subject of a great deal of research — and a lot of 
talk in the media — over the past 20 years or so. Does solving a puzzle a day 
keep dementia at bay? The short answer? Maybe.
And now for the long answer. Dementia is a blanket term for more than 100 
conditions, all of which involve a gradual deterioration in thinking, memory, 
and everyday abilities. Science doesn’t completely understand the causes of 
dementia — at least not yet!
The experts are still studying how dementia develops and progresses, and 
whether humans can do anything to delay or even prevent it. The scientific 
results aren’t straightforward — human biology rarely is — and the results 
are varied.
In general, as we humans age, our brains shrink, and our mental abilities get 
worse (though this doesn’t necessarily lead to dementia). However, recent 
research shows that you can prevent and maybe even reverse this decline by 
being physically active and giving yourself mental challenges.
In some studies, people improved their abilities in whatever aspect of mental 
training they were working on — so someone who was practicing memory 
skills, for example, showed improvements in memory, but not much improve-
ment in speed of processing or reasoning.

12
Part I: Working with Word Searches 
Physical changes happen within your brain when you learn something new. 
Recent research has revealed that this flexibility or plasticity exists for your 
entire life span. It’s your brain’s lifelong ability to rearrange neural pathways 
based on new experiences.
Other recent findings show that humans have lifetime memory — so if you 
once played guitar or spoke French in your youth, picking those activities 
back up can reactivate those circuits in your brain, and you can regain those 
abilities relatively quickly. You won’t end up learning from scratch.
Boosting your brain health
The following activities have been shown to 
benefit your brain’s health:
 ✓ Exercising: Gardening, walking, cycling, 
dancing, swimming — whatever you enjoy. 
One study shows that 15 minutes of aerobic 
exercise three times a week cuts the risk of 
dementia by at least a third. Even stretching 
helps.
 ✓ Training your brain: The particular aspect 
you train improves that aspect of mental 
functioning, so solving logic puzzles like 
Sudoku helps your logical thinking, for 
example. To improve your memory, play 
memory games.
 ✓ Studying a new language: Try learning how 
to count to ten in a couple of languages if a 
whole language is too daunting.
 ✓ Taking up a new hobby: Knitting, embroi-
dery, archery, camping — anything will do. 
And if it involves physical exercise benefits, 
even better!
 ✓ Learning to use your computer: Lots of 
courses are available (as well as younger 
people who know far too much about such 
contraptions for their own good).
 ✓ Cultivating an interest in art and music: Visit 
galleries, pick up a paintbrush, take a class 
or two, or listen to music. Play an instrument 
or sing if you can. If you can’t carry a tune, 
try going to concerts or the theater.
 ✓ Playing card and board games with your 
friends and family: These pastimes are 
a great social activity as well as a good 
mental activity! Chess is rated as one of 
the top brain workouts around.
 ✓ Solving puzzles: This book is a good start, 
but don’t stop here! You can do crosswords, 
cryptic crosswords, Sudoku (check out the 
Sudoku For Dummies series, published by 
Wiley), mazes, logic puzzles, and lots more.
 ✓ Reading: Widen your horizons, try new 
authors, and challenge yourself. Delve into 
the fun world of a book club at your local 
library or check out BookCrossing (www.
bookcrossing.com) — both free and 
fun activities.
 ✓ Doing jigsaw puzzles: They help with visual 
and hand-eye coordination.
 ✓ Socializing with your family and friends: 
This activity is more important than you 
may think. And it doesn’t have to involve the 
aforementioned board games — just hang 
out and chat.

 Chapter 1: Hunting through the World of Word Searches
 
Factors such as low education level, a family history of dementia, increasing 
age, head trauma, poorly controlled diabetes, stroke, smoking, and high blood 
pressure all increase your risk of developing dementia.
Sometimes, a person still develops dementia despite his best efforts. It’s not 
his fault. The cause and effect is never that straightforward.
 
Even though you may not be able to avoid dementia or Alzheimer’s entirely, 
you can probably stave off the onset and hopefully reduce the severity of the 
disease by being mentally active.
Research into dementia will continue, with new theories, studies, and discov-
eries hopefully leading to new treatments. While scientists work away on the 
mysteries of the brain, you can work away on the mysteries in this book and 
discover some benefits for yourself!
Considering the importance 
of mind puzzles and games
What you’re looking for here is getting out of that rut. Doing something the way 
you’ve always done it seems to weaken the brain’s wiring. Changes to your 
routines at work, school, and home always help, no matter what age you are.
Switching things up doesn’t need to be terribly hard — even changing the 
way you normally drive to work helps your neural pathways grow and 
strengthen.
The four qualities to look for in the brain-friendly activities of your choice are
 
✓ Novelty: New is better! If you’ve only worked traditional word searches, 
try your hand at the nontraditional types. (See the earlier section “Word 
Searches at a Glance” for more info.)
 
✓ Stimulation: Activities that challenge you force you to concentrate. Look 
for word searches that use words you’re unfamiliar with, involve solving 
extra puzzles like scrambled words or quizzes, or have odd shapes or 
challenging directions.
 
✓ Escalation: Activities that become increasingly difficult allow you to 
work up to higher levels of achievement. As you master a particular diffi-
culty level for the word searches in this book, move up to the next level.
 
✓ Reward: Rewards improve learning and memory at a biochemical level. 
The sense of satisfaction you get from solving a difficult word search 
puzzle or discovering a hidden message is just one of the rewards you 
have to look forward to.

Part I: Working with Word Searches 
A Fun Romp through Word 
Search History
There isn’t a lot of information available on the history of word search puzzles. 
Here’s what I’ve been able to piece together; don’t say I never gave you anything.
Around 1860, puzzle books of the day started to publish a new puzzle. A 
phrase, short poem, or other piece of writing (generally something frightfully 
educational and moralistic) was written along a path that was folded up to 
make a grid of letters, a bit like a maze. The paths were generally very regular, 
and each word came after the other in sequence. Diagonal movement was a 
no-no in these puzzles, and the letters didn’t overlap.
You can try one of these puzzles for yourself in Figure 1-1. I wrote this puzzle in 
an old-fashioned style; you can find other puzzles like it in the book Everybody’s 
Illustrated Book of Puzzles, by Don Lemon (Kessinger Publishing), originally 
published in 1890 in London. Start at the middle, with the big letter A and work 
your way out from there. You can find a hint in Chapter 12, and the answer is in 
Chapter 13 if you get stuck!

Proverbs within a Maze
This is a kind of 
maze. Find the first 
letter of the first 
word, and then 
follow on till you 
have solved the 
secret. You may 
read from one letter 
to the next, north, 
south, east or west, 
but never in a 
diagonal direction. 
You will find here a 
small collection of 
proverbs which, if 
you heed, will be as 
useful to you as they 
have been to many 
others before you.

15
 Chapter 1: Hunting through the World of Word Searches
Another early version of the word search was known as Word Chess, Knight’s 
Tour Letter Puzzles, or Knight’s Tour Crypts. The letters of the phrase or 
sentence in this sort of puzzle run along the path a knight in chess takes (two 
squares forward, one to the left or right; or one forward, two to the left or 
right). Very convoluted! You can still find these puzzles around in puzzle 
aficionados’ magazines and Web sites.
Pinning down the first contemporary word search puzzle (with a set of words 
randomly placed in a grid of letters) is very difficult. Several people in several 
countries seem to have invented it independently around the same time.
The prolific Spanish puzzle writer Pedro Ocón de Oro, who designed around 
125 different types of puzzles, invented Sopa de letras (“Soup of Letters”), 
probably sometime in the 1960s. This creation was a true word search puzzle 
with a word list and letter grid.
Norman E. Gibat from Norman, Oklahoma, seems to have been the first person 
to design a word search in English. He came up with the puzzle as a way of 
attracting readers to his small advertising publication, the Selenby Digest 
(“Sell and Buy,” get it?). He put the names of 34 Oklahoman cities and towns 
in a 20 x 20 grid. He placed the words vertically, horizontally, and diagonally 
like they typically appear today, and his puzzle contained overlapping letters. 
It was published on March 1, 1968.
This new puzzle quickly became the most interesting item in the Digest (ads 
versus a puzzle — no contest!), and Gibat’s readers clamored for more. The 
town’s teachers quickly discovered these newfangled word searches and 
gave them to their students, establishing a long classroom tradition.
Two years later, French-Canadian Jo Ouellet developed the Wonderword 
puzzle, a themed word search whose leftover letters reveal a hidden word 
(see Chapter 5 for puzzles like this one). Her son David Ouellet has been writ-
ing these puzzles professionally for many years.
The word search’s popularity has spread ever since: Word searches in a wide 
range of languages appear in ever-increasing varieties in classrooms, puzzle 
books, magazines, newspapers, and online under many different names.
Word search puzzles seem to be widespread in the Latin/Roman languages but 
are not common in Asian languages. The Spanish still solve Sopa de letras, the 
Swedish play Sökord, Germans unravel Wörterraten, the French solve Mots 
Caches, the Dutch enjoy Woordzoeker, Afrikaaners play Woordsoek-raaisels, 
and in Finland they have Sanaverkko, just to name a few.
One thing is for certain — if you love these puzzles, you’re in good company!

16
Part I: Working with Word Searches 

Chapter 2
Perfecting Your Puzzle-Solving 
Strategies 
In This Chapter
▶ Getting ready to solve word searches
▶ Discovering strategies that can help you with other word games
I
f you open almost any newspaper or magazine, you’re sure to find a word 
search puzzle or two of some kind. They’re popular puzzles for kids and 
adults alike, plus they’re a good way to extend your vocabulary. Perfect for 
those times when you want to solve a puzzle without the mental stress of 
crossword clues or putting numbers in boxes!
In this chapter, I show you the various strategies for solving word search 
puzzles. I also include some hints and tips on how to work with random 
assortments of letters to make words — the sort of skills you need when you 
play games like Scrabble, Boggle, Upwords, and other anagram-type games.
Solving Word Searches
A word search puzzle is simply a collection of words hidden in a grid of seem-
ingly random letters. A list of words to find appears next to the letter grid; 
in the puzzle, the words (almost) always run in a straight line horizontally, 
vertically, or diagonally in two directions; they read forward or backward in 
each of these four directions. This all adds up to eight different directions 
that words can be found.
Word searches range from small grids with easy-to-find words to large grids 
with challenging words — unusual spellings, short words — or even extra 
words in the word list that don’t appear in the puzzle! Some puzzles have 
additional layers, like hidden messages; others are more difficult because the 
puzzle writer gives you no word list, scrambles up the words, or is otherwise 
fiendish, making you work that much harder!

18
Part I: Working with Word Searches 
 
Word search puzzles come in a seemingly endless number of varieties; although 
the basic solving strategies in this section are usually the same for each kind of 
puzzle, each type can also present its own challenges. Check out the chapters 
in Parts II and III for any additional instructions that apply to a specific type of 
puzzle.
 
Every single puzzle in this book has a hint; if you get really stuck, don’t give 
up and head to the answers right away — give the hints section a try (see 
Chapter 12). This section is my chance to whisper over your shoulder and 
encourage you to keep going! Nothing beats the satisfaction of solving a 
difficult puzzle all by yourself (or with a little help), and I know you can 
do it.
Gathering your equipment
All you need to do a word search is a way of marking the words you’ve found 
in the grid. Because some words overlap and letters may be used more than 
once, the main goal here is to use a writing instrument that doesn’t obscure 
the letters.
Though a crayon may be the handiest tool if you live with young children, the 
simplest choice is a pencil or pen. A pencil and eraser are advisable if you’re 
dealing with a more difficult puzzle.
 
My favorite tool when solving word searches is a highlighter pen. Highlighters 
help you easily see where each word is, and they don’t obscure the letters. 
I think yellow is the easiest color to see through, but you can find plenty of 
other colors — use whatever tickles your fancy!
Finally, a dictionary, other reference books, or even some Web sites may be 
helpful with some harder puzzles.
Marking the words
After you pick a marking tool, you have to figure out how you’re going to 
mark the words themselves after you’ve found them. Keep in mind that some 
letters in the grid are used in two or three words, so you don’t want to com-
pletely obscure them when you’re marking words in the grid. This section 
gives you some options.
The highlighter pen works well with overlapping letters because you can 
see the underlying letters clearly. When a few letters overlap, the ink is a bit 
darker in that section. This method also makes finding leftover letters easier. 
See Figure 2-1 for an example.

If the paper your puzzle is printed on is thin, the highlighter may bleed through 
to the other side. Test the pen on the margin first so you don’t accidentally 
mess up another puzzle’s grid.
If you prefer to use a pen or pencil, simply draw a loop around each word as 
you find it. Remember: This way of marking the words can sometimes make 
locating leftover letters a bit confusing, so be sure to look extra carefully! 
You can also circle the letters of the words you find one-by-one. This method 
makes it easier to see hidden word messages because the leftover letters 
stand out better. I demonstrate both methods in Figure 2-2.

Part I: Working with Word Searches 
You may also like to simply draw a light line through the words in pencil or 
colored pencil. This method can work well, as long as you can still see the 
letters. Because of overlapping words, you may need those letters in another 
word before the puzzle is done.
Tackling the grid
Let’s face it, word searches are, well, fairly simple puzzles, and there’s not a 
huge amount of strategy involved in solving them. However, there are some 
tricks of the trade:
 
✓ Scan the grid lines either up and down columns (see Figure 2-3), 
or backward and forward across rows, line by line (see Figure 2-4). 
Going through a grid both ways generally helps you catch anything 
you missed on the first pass. You may find that running a finger or the 
end of your pen (not the ink end!) along the line helps you keep your 
place.
 
✓ If you’re feeling very focused, you can try the speed search method 
and scan up and down two columns of letters at a time. (Figure 2-3 also 
shows you an example of this method.) Use your finger to help you keep 
place. I find this method works best when scanning the grid vertically 
and not along rows, but if it works for you, go for it.

✓ Look for the first letter of the word you’re searching for. When you 
find it, run your finger around the letter in a circle check, looking at each 
of the letter’s eight neighbors.
 
 In Figure 2-5, for example, you’re looking for the word PANCAKE. Search 
along the rows or down the columns for the letter P. When you find a P, 
stop for a second and look at the eight letters around it for the second 
letter (A). If you find an A, look in that same direction for an N (because 
the word must be in a straight line) and continue until you find the whole 
word. You can see here that three As appear next to this P, but only one 
then follows on to an N and then the rest of the word PANCAKE.
 
✓ Simply stare at the grid until words pop out at you. This method may 
sound ridiculous, but it can be surprisingly effective, especially for 
longer words and those that run forward. This method can get you some 
of the words but probably won’t reveal all of them.
 
✓ Look for rarer letters that occur within a word. The letters Z, Q, J, X, K, 
V, B, and W are (in descending order) the least frequently used letters 
in the English language, so they probably appear less often in the letter 
grid. If the word you’re searching for is SOJOURN, you may find it more 
quickly by looking for the J rather than starting at the S.
 
 However, if the puzzle writer is being particularly devious (why are you 
looking at me like that?), she may counterbalance this rare letter effect 
by using plenty of these letters in the grid fill — the letters that are 
added to complete the grid after the words have been placed.

✓ Watch for certain letters that may pop out at you more than others, 
especially circular letters. You may find that O, Q, C, and X are easier to 
see in the grid.
 
✓ Look for other patterns in the words, especially double letters. Scan 
your word list for words with double letters — LL, EE, SS, OO, and TT 
are the most common ones — and then search for these in the grid.
 
✓ Focus on the shapes letters make instead of thinking of them as let-
ters, and look for the visual patterns. This strategy kicks the creative, 
pattern-recognizing right side of your brain into gear and forces that 
bossy, logical left side to take a back seat.
Moving Past Word Searches: Anagrams 
and Other Word Games
You may be struggling to find a word — any word, let alone a high scor-
ing one — in Scrabble or Upwords. Or your partner may keep beating you 
at Boggle (in our house, the kids wrote “No Daddy!” on the box). Perhaps 
WordScraper on Facebook or the Jumble puzzle in the paper is driving you 
nuts. Maybe you keep coming to a sticky end in Hangman.
Never fear — help is at hand. In the following sections, I give you a crash 
course in anagrams, including some of their history and a whole bunch of 
patterns and strategies that can help you improve your game skills and blitz 
the opposition!

23
 Chapter 2: Perfecting Your Puzzle-Solving Strategies
Understanding anagrams
An anagram is one or more words created from the letters of another word. 
One example is GARDEN and DANGER — they have the same set of six letters 
rearranged to make different words.
Anagrams have been around for thousands of years. The Ancient Greeks 
were particularly fond of a good anagram, and in the Middle Ages an anagram 
of a person’s name was thought to reveal her true nature and future. Some 
authors have written under anagrams of their real names.
Anagramming as a party game and puzzling pastime reached the height of 
its popularity in the Victorian era; Lewis Carroll especially championed the 
form. Anagrams that create particularly clever or appropriate messages are 
highly prized. Did you know LIONESSES are NOISELESS? And MARRIAGE 
can be A GRIM ERA. Perhaps that last one is an antigram — an anagram that 
results in a phrase opposed to the meaning of the starting words, like turning 
ASTRONOMERS into NO MORE STARS.
You may be familiar with anagrams in cryptic crosswords, Jumble puzzles, 
Scrabble, Boggle, and some TV game shows. Some computer puzzle adven-
ture games have anagram puzzles to solve. Plenty of word fans enjoy creating 
clever anagrams, and you can find these folks and their wordplay online if 
you get hooked!
The excellent program Zyzzyva is perfect if you want to play around with 
anagrams and other word games. It’s helpful for solving all the puzzles in 
Chapter 11, as well as the Scrambled Word Searches in Chapter 7. You can 
download it for free at www.zyzzyva.net.
These puzzles are a bit different from the others in the book, and all have one 
thing in common — you need to discover words within a random set of let-
ters. You know these games all too well, I’m sure!
Anagramming tricks
The classic game of Anagrams is a fun pen and paper game — simply start 
with a word or sentence and, using just those letters (no repeats allowed), 
turn them into another set of words.
You can reach the heights of this game by creating related (and generally 
funny or apt) names or phrases. For example, the letters of THE MONA LISA 
also cleverly spell out NO HAT, A SMILE.

24
Part I: Working with Word Searches 
When you’re faced with an unruly collection of letters — whether in a 
straight anagram, a cryptic crossword clue, or a set of tiles in a game — some 
of the following tricks should help you tame them:
 
✓ Write the anagram backward or in a circle. Basically, mix up the letters 
more. This tactic is especially helpful if you’re starting with a word that 
you need to turn into another word (such as going from EGRET to GREET).
 
✓ Divide the letters into vowels and consonants. This technique works 
well for long anagrams.
 
✓ Write the scrambled letters out in alphabetical order.
 
✓ Look for common patterns that you can make with the set of letters 
you’ve got. SH, CH, TH, HE, AN, RE, IE, LL, and EE can get you started; 
see Chapter 14 for a full collection.
 
✓ Expand your vocabulary so you know more words. Even learning just 
one new word a day makes a difference. You can subscribe to free online 
services at
 
• A.Word.A.Day: wordsmith.org/awad
 
• Dictionary.com: dictionary.reference.com
 
• Merriam-Webster: www.merriam-webster.com/cgi-bin/
mwwod.pl
 
• Oxford Dictionaries: www.askoxford.com
See what you can find with the letters of your own name, using the tips in 
this section. For instance, my name (DENISE SUTHERLAND) can spell out 
DEADLINE, SHUN REST, which seems incredibly appropriate at the moment! 
To try your hand at some created puzzles, head to Chapter 11.
Rearranging letters for tile games
Some word games, such as Scrabble and Upwords, depend on rearranging 
letters to form words. In these games, you have no guarantee that the set of 
letters in any given hand can form a good collection of words — you may be 
struggling with only one vowel (or nothing but vowels). The following list 
gives you some helpful hints for working with what you’ve got:
 
✓ Keep shuffling your letter tiles to see what patterns fall out.
 
✓ Take into account the available letters on the board.
 
✓ Look for common letter combinations such as TH, HE, AN, RE, SH, ON, 
ST, and TR (see Chapter 14 for a full list).
 
✓ Suffixes such as ER, ED, ING, and IER are helpful. So are prefixes such as 
DE, UN, PRE, and IN.

25
 Chapter 2: Perfecting Your Puzzle-Solving Strategies
 
✓ Some letters are more likely to occur at the start or end of a word. For 
example, B and W are more likely to appear at the beginning of a word 
than the end, and Y and E are more commonly end letters than starting 
letters.
 
✓ Oh, did I mention? Keep moving those tiles!
Note: This book isn’t a guide on how to become a champion Scrabble player 
or even get high scores. For that sort of training, I suggest you look for one 
of the many excellent books on the market or search the Internet — you 
can join a host of good Web sites and groups. The Official Scrabble Players 
Dictionary (Merriam-Webster) and Joe Edley and John D. Williams Jr.’s book 
Everything Scrabble (Pocket) provide expert knowledge.
Baffling letter squares
What about games like Boggle that come with the instruction to “make as 
many words as you can using these letters”? These games often have arbi-
trary rules to make things harder, like outlawing two- or three-letter words 
or requiring every word to use the central letter in the grid. And if you’re 
playing it on the Internet, no doubt you have a time limit too. Infuriating!
Whether you’re playing Boggle or a similar version of the game that’s popular 
in newspapers, the following tips can help you get the most out of those few 
letters:
 
✓ Get the rules clear in your mind. Are you allowed to move diagonally? 
What is the minimum word length? Does the central letter have to be 
included in every word? Are proper nouns allowed?
 
✓ Focus on a quarter of the grid at a time. Look at each of the letters in 
turn and do a circle check (as much as possible, even if it’s just a half 
circle). Follow up any good combinations to see whether they lead to 
words.
 
✓ Move on to the next quarter and repeat until you’ve surveyed the 
whole board. This systematic rotation helps you ensure that you’re not 
missing anything.
You may prefer to get a good general look at the grid of letters and work by 
feel rather than in the methodical way described in the preceding list. This 
more informal approach can be a better way of finding long words in the grid. 
Here are a few pointers:
 
✓ When you find a word, look for plurals and variations (PLAY, PLAYS, 
PLAYED).
 
✓ Look for vowels and search around them to make words. Do the same 
with the most common consonants (T, N, R, S, and H).

26
Part I: Working with Word Searches 
 
✓ Look for longer words around a shorter word (CAT can expand to 
LOCATE) and short words within a longer word (DEPEND breaks down 
into PEND, PEN, and END).
 
✓ Look for rhymes (SIT, BIT, FIT, HIT, KIT, KNIT, and so on).
 
✓ Look for suffixes like ED, ER, IER, ING, and TION and prefixes like RE, DE, 
UN, IN, and AN.
 
✓ Look for two-letter patterns (such as ST, SH, STR, SP, TR, TH, PR, and 
DR) at the beginning of words.
 
✓ Look around an E for Vowel–Consonant–Silent E patterns like ATE, ORE, 
and ISE/IZE.
 
✓ Know the most common letter patterns; you can find a list in Chapter 14.
 
✓ Widen your vocabulary (see the “Anagramming tricks” section earlier in 
the chapter for a list of word-of-the-day e-mail services).
Staying alive in Hangman
My main advice for Hangman is to know the list of the most frequently used 
letters (E, T, A, O, N, R, I, S, H, and D, in descending order) and the common 
English-word letter patterns — the ones in Chapter 14 (you knew I was going 
to say that, didn’t you?).
For almost all words (3 to 15 letters long), your best first guess is either E or 
I (better minds than mine have done all the tricky computing to figure this 
out; check out www.sharkfeeder.com/hangman if you’re interested in the 
details). If it’s a two-letter word, guess A.
Of course, you can use this information against your opponent — select 
shorter words that don’t use the top ten letters. The least frequently used let-
ters in English are G, Y, P, W, B, V, K, X, J, Q, and Z (the least-used of all).
Hangman is yet another game where a wide vocabulary makes all the differ-
ence. Confront your opponent with FOX, JIVE, or EPOXY, and you’ll soon be 
the undisputed Hangman Supremo!

Part II
Starting with 
Straightforward 
Puzzles 

In this part . . .
T
his is what you’ve been waiting for — the puzzles! 
This part has the straightforward puzzles: the Simple 
and Story Word Searches, the Hidden Words, and the 
Secret Shapes. Each puzzle chapter contains Easy, 
Medium, and Hard puzzles. Work through each chapter 
in order to build your skill set and face the challenge of 
increasingly difficult puzzles. Or jump around within this 
part to try different puzzle varieties, difficulty levels, or 
themes to suit your mood.

Chapter 3
Trying Your Hand at Simple 
Word Searches
T
hese puzzles are the plain-vanilla word searches. The words are scat-
tered through the letter grid, running in straight lines in any of the eight 
directions. After you find them all, you’re done!
Some harder puzzles of this type have extra words in the word lists that don’t 
appear in the grids. Your challenge is to not only find all the words in the grid 
but also identify the words that aren’t there. I tell you how many words are 
extra so you know when to stop looking.
 
If you get stuck at any point, head to Chapter 12 for a hint — I include a hint 
there for every puzzle in this chapter. After you complete each puzzle, you can 
check your answers against the answers in Chapter 13.

Chapter 4
Reading up on Story Word 
Searches
I
n this sort of puzzle, the words are still scattered through the letter grid 
as in a Simple Word Searches (see Chapter 3), but you have to extract the 
word list from a short, and hopefully interesting, story or article. The words 
you need to find are in bold.
 
Consider the following list of tips to help you with the puzzles in this chapter:
 
✓ When two bold words occur one after the other, look for them as a 
single entry in the puzzle.
 
✓ If you prefer to work from a list, you can write out the bold words on a 
scrap of paper first. Otherwise, simply find the words in the order they 
appear in the story.
 
✓ It can help to keep track of which words you’ve found — and which ones 
remain to be discovered — by crossing them off in the story.

58
Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 28: Old Gold
Difficulty Level: Easy
Ah, chocolate — one of the major food groups . . . where would humanity be without it? 
Whether you love white, milk, dark, or bittersweet chocolate, you’re sure to find the 
perfect delicious treat. And if you buy Fair Trade, your money buys more than just 
chocolate!
Ancient Mesoamericans first discovered the cocoa tree in the Amazon rainforest thou-
sands of years ago and used the bitter water they made from its seeds in ceremonies. 
Spanish conquistadors brought the cacao seeds back to Spain, assuring chocolate’s 
reign over the world.
Initially chocolate had to be prepared by hand — a lengthy and difficult process. The 
resulting oily, gritty paste was used to make an expensive beverage for royalty and 
the wealthy.
The Industrial Revolution changed all that. Doret and Dubuisson in France and Van 
Houten in the Netherlands invented machines that made grinding and pressing the 
cacao seeds into paste and cocoa powder amazingly fast. The paste was also beautifully 
smooth and creamy and could be formed into chocolate bars.
Daniel Peter and Henri Nestlé worked together to add condensed milk to chocolate, 
creating milk chocolate. Rodolphe Lindt developed a blending technique called 
conching, which created an even smoother texture and taste.
Finally, chocolate was within the reach of everyone, and it became wildly popular. The 
mass-production of sweets, candy bars, and chocolate bars hasn’t let up. Hershey, Mars, 
Cadbury, Nestlé, and Lindt have been around since the Industrial Revolution and are still 
the biggest producers today.
Most of the world’s cocoa is now grown in West Africa, especially in Côte d’Ivoire. It’s 
a labor-intensive crop — workers tend, harvest, dry, and pack the plants and seeds by 
hand.
Cacao farmers often receive very little profit from their crops. Some try to stay afloat by 
using child labor or even returning to the bad old days of slavery. Fair Trade cocoa is a 
system that offers farmers a better way of making a living, helps break the poverty cycle, 
and stops abusive practices.

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 29: Little Mary
Difficulty Level: Easy
Gladys Louise Smith was the first international film star. You may know her better as 
Mary Pickford or “America’s Sweetheart.” The Canadian was born in 1892 and started 
acting at a young age. In 1907, Gladys was signed to appear on Broadway with New York 
producer David Belsaco. He insisted that she change her name, so Mary Pickford was 
born.
In 1910, Mary made the move to the silent silver screen. She auditioned for filmmaker 
D.W. Griffith of Biograph Studio, and was hired immediately. Over the years Pickford 
worked with Carl Laemmle’s IMP company and Adolph Zukor’s Famous Players Film 
Company. She was one of the four founders of the United Artists movie studio in 1919, 
along with Douglas Fairbanks, Charlie Chaplin, and D. W. Griffith. Eventually Mary was 
involved with many levels of the movie making process, including writing, directing, and 
producing.
By age 24, Mary Pickford was the most famous and popular woman in the world because 
of the spread of American movies internationally. It was easy to adapt silent movies for 
the worldwide market — they just had to change the language on the subtitles that 
provided text for the movie!
Mary played appealing, sympathetic characters, and her outgoing and sincere perfor-
mances struck a chord with audiences worldwide. She almost always played children or 
young adults, even when she was in her 20s. Mary and her writer friend Frances Marion 
invented cinematographic devices — such as tricks of perspective, oversized props and 
sets, and casting tall actors — to sustain the illusion.
In 1927 Mary released her last silent film, My Best Girl. In 1928, Mary cut off her famous 
long blond curls, and took on serious dramatic roles. Her first talkie movie was Coquette, 
which won her the Best Actress Oscar in 1929. The Taming of the Shrew was also released 
that year. Mary completed three more films after this — Forever Yours (1930), Kiki (1931), 
and Secrets (1933) — but none was a success. She retired from acting when she was 41, 
but remained involved in Hollywood for many years.
Mary Pickford was an incredibly influential actress — she instituted many standards 
for actors in the fledgling movie industry, established and ran a major film production 
company, and starred in over 50 feature-length films, including The Poor Little Rich Girl, 
Pollyanna, Little Lord Fauntleroy, Rebecca of Sunnybrook Farm, M’Liss, Sparrows, and 
Little Annie Rooney.

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 30: It’s Easy Being Green
Difficulty Level: Easy
If you’re anything like me, you may be frightened by the impact climate change is having 
on the world. Because the problem is so huge, you may think you can’t do anything to 
make a difference. But you can make plenty of practical and simple changes that won’t 
break the bank and will make a difference. The collective action of thousands and even 
millions of people all making some positive changes can affect the whole planet.
The big picture clearly shows that using renewable energy sources is vital — 
hydroelectricity, solar power, tidal energy, wind power, and geothermal energy 
are some options here. In many places, you can choose the source of your domestic 
electricity. You can use many solar-powered devices around the home — garden 
lights, lanterns, and even small portable solar chargers for cellphones, MP3 players, 
and laptops.
Try a low-carbon diet, choosing foods that result in a smaller carbon footprint (envi-
ronmental impact). Become a locavore: Get more of your food from the local farmers’ 
markets, and eat seasonal produce. This change reduces the number of food miles 
(the distance food is transported from producer to consumer) produce has to travel to 
your door. Buy foods with less packaging and processing. Buy Fair Trade coffee, tea, 
chocolate, and so on where possible.
Try using pencils (the sort with graphite lead inside a wooden case) rather than 
disposable pens. The pencils are not only biodegradable, but they can also draw 
about 18 times further than a pen. A fountain pen with a refillable ink bladder is even 
better (and more stylish).
Save water by taking shorter showers, and don’t let the tap run when you wash your 
hands or brush your teeth. Drink tap water rather than bottled water. You can filter it if 
you’re concerned about water quality, but you help to keep billions of disposable bottles 
out of landfill by using a cup or your own water bottle. And bottled water can be more 
expensive than gasoline!
Join Freecycle.org or BookCrossing.com. Discover your local library. Grow some of 
your own food. Walk, ride a bike, or take the bus rather than the car. Use your own 
coffee mug if you can when you go to your local coffee shop. Receive and pay your bills 
electronically. Recycle paper. Buy secondhand clothes and fabrics made from organic 
cotton, hemp blends, and bamboo. Make the change to low-energy fluorescent light 
bulbs. Bottom line: You can do lots of things that will have an immediate impact — pick 
a couple and start today!

Puzzle 31: All Awash with Watercolors
Difficulty Level: Easy
If you get bitten by the watercolor bug (you’ll know if you do — it’s beautifully colored 
and entrancing), you’ll discover a whole world of equipment as well as a lifetime of 
learning how water and color flow and interact on paper!
Watercolor paints are made from finely ground pigments mixed with gum arabic, glycerin, 
and a wetting agent. They come in various forms: tubes, cakes (also called pans), liquids, 
and pencils. Tube paint is easier to mix and good for large washes. Cake paints give you 
more immediate access and are great for portable painting and painting details.
Paints come in a wide range of colors with evocative names, such as Gamboge, Viridian, 
Cerulean Blue, Phthalo Violet, Cadmium Scarlet, Burnt Umber, Ultramarine Blue, Indigo, 
Raw Sienna, Cobalt Violet, Indian Red, and Prussian Blue. Different paints have different 
characteristics — such as being transparent or opaque and staining or non-staining — 
and different amounts of sediment.
Watercolor brushes come in a wide range of shapes, fibers, and styles. A rigger has long 
fibers and is great for producing thin lines. A flat brush has hairs in a flat rectangle and can 
range in width from quite narrow to several inches wide for painting flat washes. Round 
brushes are versatile, so you want to have them in a range of sizes. When choosing round 
brushes, make sure they come to a sharp point and hold a good amount of paint.
Kolinsky sable brushes are the very best. Oddly, their fibers come from the tails of 
Siberian martens, not sables! Other fibers choices include ox hair, badger hair, camel 
hair, goat hair, Chinese hog bristle, and a wide range of synthetics. Some brushes mix 
sable with synthetics to provide the benefits of a sable brush at a lower cost.
Watercolor paper comes in a range of surfaces. Hot pressed paper is fine-grained and 
smooth; rough paper has — you guessed it — a very rough surface and gives a lot of 
texture to a painting. Cold or not-pressed paper falls somewhere in the middle, with a 
slight texture to the surface. Look for archival quality paper (with a neutral pH and made 
from permanent fibers such as cotton), and your gorgeous artwork will last forever!

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 32: Il Menù Per Favore
Difficulty Level: Medium
Italian cuisine has been around since antiquity and is one of the most appetizing and 
best-known cuisines in the world. After all, what town doesn’t have a pizzeria? But Italy 
has so much more to offer than just pizza, lasagne, and spaghetti Bolognese! Every 
region has its own specialties.
Italian cuisine is full of rich and delicious dishes — try risottos made with Arborio rice, 
frittatas made with eggs and vegetables, polenta, stews like osso buco and buridda, and 
soups like minestrone. Chewy Italian bread dipped in a fragrant extra-virgin olive oil is a 
wonderful meal in itself.
Olive oil is a basic ingredient in Italian food. Herbs and other seasonings common in 
Italian cooking include garlic, flat-leafed parsley, sweet balsamic vinegar, capers, ancho-
vies, oregano, sage, thyme, basil, and tarragon. Sweets often use cinnamon, almond, or 
vanilla for flavor.
But no dessert until you finish your meal! Antipasti are the traditional way to start an 
Italian meal. A typical antipasti platter has a variety of smoked or cured meats, like pro-
sciutto or pancetta, marinated vegetables, and cheeses. You’re likely to find asparagus, 
eggplant (aubergine), sun-dried tomatoes, porcini mushrooms, fennel, bell peppers 
(capsicum), and artichokes. And don’t forget the olives, both green and black!
Parmigiano-Reggiano cheese from northern Italy (also known as Parmesan) has been 
a stock ingredient in many kitchens for ages, as has soft, stretchy mozzarella. Other 
Italian cheeses are gaining in popularity too — semi-hard provolone, the soft sheep’s 
milk whey cheese ricotta, soft bocconcini and mascarpone, the blue cheese gorgonzola, 
and caprino (made with goat’s milk), to name but a few.
Pasta is another Italian food that can be found everywhere — it’s the basis of so many 
quick, healthy meals. Macaroni, penne, spaghetti, fettuccine, farfalle, and vermicelli 
(“little worms”) are just a few of the shapes available. Gnocchi are tender dumplings 
made from potato, flour, semolina, or a combination thereof.
Italian cuisine doesn’t just taste good, either — many experts recommend a so-called 
Mediterranean diet high in olive oil, legumes, unrefined cereals, produce, fish, wine, 
and poultry as a healthy option. So what are you waiting for? Buon appetito!

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 33: Birthstones and Flowers
Difficulty Level: Medium
The tradition of assigning certain gemstones and flowers to each month includes the 
idea that wearing your birthstone makes you luckier. Even if you don’t believe that, 
birth flowers and birthstones are lovely gifts. Flowers also have symbolic meanings and 
are a thoughtful inclusion in a celebratory bouquet for a new baby or any birthday. Take 
a look at the flowers and gemstones for each month:
 ✓ January’s birthstone is garnet, and the birth flower is the carnation, a fragrant 
winter flower with meanings of pride, love, and beauty.
 ✓ The birthstone for February is almost always amethyst, although pearl is an option 
too; the birth flowers are iris and violet, both beautiful heralds of spring.
 ✓ March’s stone is aquamarine, and its birth flower is the daffodil, which symbolizes 
a return to warmth, rebirth, and hope.
 ✓ The stone for those born in April is the diamond; their flower is the daisy, a symbol 
of innocence, modesty, and brightness.
 ✓ May’s birthstone is the emerald. Lily of the valley is the flower, representing purity 
and a return to happiness.
 ✓ June babies’ birthstone is the pearl; their flower, symbolizing love and appreciation, 
is the rose.
 ✓ Ruby is the modern birthstone for July, but onyx was the classic stone. The flower 
is larkspur, bringing to mind summer meadows, lightness, and levity.
 ✓ The contemporary stone for August birthdays is peridot, but you also have a lot 
of choice from times past: topaz, carnelian, moonstone, alexandrite, sardonyx, 
and sapphire. Gladiolus is August’s flower, symbolizing strength of character and 
remembrance.
 ✓ For September, the stone is sapphire. Aster, a classic autumn flower that represents 
patience and daintiness, is its flower.
 ✓ October’s birthstone is opal; tourmaline, aquamarine, and beryl are other, old-
 fashioned choices. Marigold, following the sun, is October’s flower.
 ✓ The stone for November is topaz. Chrysanthemum is the month’s flower, symboliz-
ing cheer and joy, compassion, and friendship.
 ✓ Turquoise is the birthstone for those born in December, although ruby and blood-
stone were older choices. The fragrant narcissus, a symbol of peace, is December’s 
flower.

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 34: Knitting with a Twist
Difficulty Level: Medium
Knitting has never really gone away, but it has been hiding for a while. Now it’s back and 
reinvigorated by an unexpected source: the Internet!
The online knitting and crocheting community is huge. Women, men, kids, teens, older 
crafters, and everyone in between are getting back into the simple joys of making things 
by hand — with their computers by their sides.
One new technological invention is free online knitting and crocheting magazines. Knitty.
com is foremost among these publications — a sassy, funny, intelligent Canadian quarterly 
production with free patterns, articles, and more. Knitty was established by Amy R. Singer 
in June 2002 and has well over 30,000 readers worldwide every day.
Thousands, if not millions, of stitchers have blogs (web logs or online diaries). They share 
photos of their WIPs (Works in Progress), UFOs (Unfinished Objects), and FOs (Finished 
Objects). Experienced crafters often put up free patterns or write tutorials on their blogs. 
Knitting authors such as Stephanie Pearl-McPhee (The Yarn Harlot) have risen to great 
prominence through blogging, in addition to their wonderful knitting books.
Some folks offer online knitalongs, in which they decide to knit a particular pattern, say, 
a pair of socks or a lace shawl, and invite others to knit it at the same time. Knitalongs 
often have their own blogs, where members share any difficulties they’re having with the 
project, post photos of their progress, and generally join in and help each other despite 
being scattered across the globe!
Ravelry.com, set up by Casey and Jessica Forbes in May 2007, is another online outlet for 
fiber crafters. It’s an online community where you can keep track of your projects, yarn 
stash, books and magazines, and inventory of needles and hooks, as well as find kindred 
crafting souls. Through Ravelry’s pattern and yarn directory, you can see what other 
people have made with a particular yarn or how their version of a pattern turned out.
All this online networking also leads to more get-togethers in person and an increasing 
circle of friends. Most cities and towns have active knitting groups that meet regularly in 
cafés, pubs, libraries, and other public places. If you can’t find one of those, join World 
Wide Knit in Public Day, the brainchild of Danielle Landes. It’s the largest knitter-run 
public activity in the world, held each year on the second Saturday in June. So if you see 
knitters and crocheters around, don’t be scared by the pointy sticks — come on over 
and join in!

71

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 35: You Say Tomato
Difficulty Level: Medium
When the Pilgrims left England for the New World in 1620, they took with them Elizabethan 
English. In each country the languages changed, to the point where, as George Bernard 
Shaw supposedly said, England and America are “two countries divided by a common 
language.”
Many American words are pulled directly from Elizabethan English but have fallen 
from use in England. Included in this category are gotten (got in modern England), fall 
(autumn), mad (angry), platter (large dish), deck of cards (pack of cards), and trash 
(rubbish), to name but a few.
Many words sneered at in Great Britain as “vulgar Americanisms” are in fact British in 
origin and not American at all; some of these are glee, jeopardy, smolder, and progress 
as a verb. The British use a great deal of American words and spellings, even replacing 
some of their own words almost without being aware on it. This list includes, in part, 
mask for masque, truck for lorry, jail rather than gaol, eventuate, input, commuter, 
gimmick, babysitter, lengthy, hangover, blizzard, teenager, soggy, graveyard, raincoat, 
notify, and publicity.
Many American and British words are spelled differently. Commonwealth countries — 
Canada, Australia, and New Zealand in particular — also use U.K. spelling. In general, 
they use a u in many words, where Americans have dropped it. Color and colour; humor 
and humour; honor and honour; and labor and labour are the most common examples of 
this. Other common letter substitutes are z instead of s (the U.S. finalize versus the U.K. 
finalise), -er instead of -re (theater versus theatre), and single instead of double letters 
(counselor versus counsellor).
American spelling is often simpler than its U.K. counterpart (which generally retains 
letters that reveal the original origins of a word, such as French); some examples include 
program instead of programme, catalog instead of catalogue, dialog instead of dialogue, 
and omelet instead of omelette. American aluminum is aluminium in England (and 
everywhere else in the world). Other differences include gray instead of grey, ax instead 
of axe, and inquire instead of enquire.
The languages also have many differences in idiom, which can trip up the unwary traveler. 
Just one example: a biscuit in America is a scone in the U.K., while a biscuit in the U.K. is a 
cookie in America. The main thing to remember is that while these two varieties of English 
are different in many ways, they have more in common than may at first be apparent, and 
neither one is better than the other!

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 36: Trick or Treat!
Difficulty Level: Medium
Halloween is one of the few ancient festivals that is getting more popular around the world. 
It’s already well established, in some form or other, in the United Kingdom, Ireland, the 
United States, Canada, Mexico, Northern Italy, and Romania. In recent years it has become 
popular in Japan, Germany, Spain, and some South American countries; it hasn’t been as 
widespread in Australia and New Zealand, but it’s slowly becoming more accepted.
The conflicting theories about the origins of Halloween are difficult to verify after thou-
sands of years. The first text records of Halloween are of the night before All Saints’ Day 
in Scotland in the 700s. Though it was something of a sidenote to that holiday, eventually 
it became a festival in its own right. As time went on, Scottish children started to dress 
up and go door-to-door asking for money and sweet treats. Halloween eventually spread 
from Scotland to England, Wales, and Ireland in the 1800s. Love divinations — trying 
to discover who your future husband or wife would be with mirrors, apple peels, and 
roasted nuts — were a popular Halloween pastime in Victorian times.
Halloween nearly died out in the United Kingdom and Ireland, but it was revived in North 
America by Irish immigrants in the late 1800s. And boy, did it ever take off! Halloween is 
now big business, as well as a lot of fun. No wonder it’s an increasingly popular festival: 
Dressing up in a costume and going trick-or-treating is a highlight of any child’s — and 
many an adult’s — year. After all, what other time of year can you dress up as a ghost, 
mummy, witch, skeleton, demon, superhero, zombie, elf, alien, angel, fairy, pirate, or 
anything else without attracting a second glance?
Some cities and towns hold Halloween parades. Folks decorate their houses with harvest 
or scary themes (especially in colors like soot black, bone white, and pumpkin orange): 
scarecrows, pumpkins, skeletons, black cats, cobwebs with huge spiders, and bats in 
the rafters are all popular. Countless pumpkins are sacrificed as jack-o’-lanterns. You 
can go on ghost tours, visit haunted houses, stay warm around a bonfire, watch horror 
movies, read scary stories, get messy with apple bobbing, drink hot apple cider, and get 
a bellyache from too much sweet stuff! Candy corn, pumpkin pie, candy apples, popcorn, 
and Halloween-colored candies are some of the treats up for grabs.

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 37: Tip of the Slung
Difficulty Level: Medium
Spoonerisms are slips of the tongue where the first parts of two words (or syllables within 
one word) are swapped. You may commit one of these verbal slipups accidentally or on 
purpose as fun wordplay. For example, a butterfly turns into a flutterby, and the speed of 
light becomes the lead of spite. The technical term for this transposition is metaphasis.
Spoonerisms get their name from the Reverend William Spooner, the Anglican priest 
and scholar who lectured in divinity, philosophy, and history at Oxford University in 
London. Spooner was a well-liked professor, if somewhat absent-minded. Though he 
does seem to have often muddled his words, many doubt that he said all of the quota-
tions attributed to him. The following are some of the classic spoonerisms:
 ✓ “Let us raise our glasses to the queer old dean” (the dear old Queen)
 ✓ “Young man, you have hissed my mystery lectures (missed my history lectures) and 
tasted two worms (wasted two terms)!”
 ✓ “Which of us has not felt in his heart a half-warmed fish (half-formed wish)?”
 ✓ “A well-boiled icicle” (A well-oiled bicycle)
 ✓ “Our shoving leopard” (Our loving shepherd)
Creating spoonerisms is a fun game to play; many Web sites and books collect clever and 
apt transpositions. Ideally, the transposed words should form proper words, not non-
sense words (for instance, growing vegetables turns into vowing gregatables). However, 
there are no hard and fast rules.
Here are a few more spoonerisms to enjoy; see if you can figure out what they’re meant 
to say. (If you get stuck, check out www.fun-with-words.com/spoon_example.html 
for some help.)
 ✓ Fighting a liar
 ✓ One swell foop
 ✓ Cattle ships and bruisers
 ✓ Tow and shell
 ✓ Have the hags flung out
 ✓ Dow the thrice
 ✓ Please hush my brat
 ✓ Logged over
 ✓ Wave the sails
 ✓ A lack of pies

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 38: How to Spot a Hoax E-mail
Difficulty Level: Hard
E-mail is a mixed blessing — it’s a wonderful way to stay in touch with family, friends, and 
colleagues, but a huge amount of rubbish comes in every day, sometimes far outweighing 
the real messages. Almost every chain e-mail, warning about a new toxin lurking in your 
home, health scare, computer virus alert, advertisement, or great new business offer is a 
hoax, scam, urban legend, or fake of some sort. Approach with caution!
Before forwarding a message, check its credentials. See whether it appears on one of the 
many hoax-busting sites such as Snopes (www.snopes.com) or Urban Legends (urban
legends.about.com). These sites have a great deal of information on how to spot the 
fakes, as well as lists of the legitimate e-mails making the rounds.
Other warning signs are mispeld tixt, ALL CAPS, and lots of exclamation marks!!!!! If the 
author pleads with you to “send this message to everyone in your address book,” you 
can automatically hit the delete key.
Never ever reply to a spam or hoax e-mail, whether to voice your annoyance or request 
to be removed from the mailing list. This mistake only serves to confirm your e-mail 
address with the spammer, and you’ll never hear the end of it.
Even if you want to buy what a spam e-mail is selling, don’t do it under any circum-
stances! Spammers use fraudulent business practices, and honestly, who wants to 
encourage them?
Phishing is a technique in which scammers try to get you to send them your banking 
details, passwords, and other personal details. These e-mails generally look like they’re 
from your bank, eBay, the IRS, and so on; they say you have a problem that needs to be 
fixed and direct you to a Web site asking you for your account information. Again, don’t 
open these e-mails or click on any links in them. How can you tell what’s phishy and what 
isn’t? One clue is that phishing e-mails don’t have your full name in the text. A real e-mail 
from your bank or eBay would be addressed to you properly.
You may also consider taking the “Boulder Pledge,” written by Roger Ebert in 1996: 
“Under no circumstances will I ever purchase anything offered to me as the result of an 
unsolicited e-mail message. Nor will I forward chain letters, petitions, mass mailings, or 
virus warnings to large numbers of others. This is my contribution to the survival of the 
online community.”
If you’re interested in finding more about avoiding spam, I recommend the Skeptoid 
podcast by Brian Dunning (skeptoid.com), and the Spam Primer by Randy Cassingham 
(www.spamprimer.com).

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 39: Hop, Skip, and Jump
Difficulty Level: Hard
In Australia, marsupials (mammals whose offspring develop in pouches) are more 
common than the placental mammals. A female marsupial’s baby is born when it’s only 
a few weeks old and still very small. It then crawls into her pouch, attaches itself to a 
nipple, and completes its growth inside the pouch.
Macropods (macro meaning “large” and pod meaning “foot”) are hopping marsupials. 
They range from the kangaroos to the smaller wallabies, tree-kangaroos, pademelons, 
and quokkas, which are only as big as a cat. All macropods are herbivorous, and most 
are nocturnal.
North America is home to only one marsupial: the possum. In Australia, however, the 
possum family is large, with around sixty species ranging from the tiny pygmy possum 
(less than five inches long) and cute sugar glider to the largest of them all, the common 
brushtail possum.
The brushtail possum has adapted to city life, sleeping in tree hollows during the day 
and visiting gardens at night. It loves sweet treats and can destroy vegetable gardens and 
fruit on trees. Males make loud territorial calls at night, grunting, coughing, screeching, 
and hissing enough to give anyone nightmares!
The bilby is an endangered long-eared, desert-burrowing marsupial. In Australia, the 
Easter Bilby is gaining popularity over the Easter Bunny — especially because rabbits 
have caused so much damage to the continent.
Other unusual Australian marsupials include the numbat, a small diurnal (opposite 
of nocturnal) striped animal that lives on termites and is the only marsupial without a 
pouch; bandicoots, which are related to bilbies; the large and solid burrowing wombats; 
two species of blind marsupial moles that burrow through the desert sands of Western 
Australia; the endangered potoroos; bettongs, also known as rat-kangaroos; the shrew-
like antechinus; the tiny mouse-like dunnarts; the feisty Tasmanian devil, the largest 
carnivorous marsupial in the world; the well-known koalas, which live on eucalyptus 
leaves and sleep for most of the day; and six species of spotted quolls, or native cats.

Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 40: Highs and Lows
Difficulty Level: Hard
Look out! There is one extra bold word in this story that isn’t in the grid — can you spot it?
Diabetes is a series of conditions that are involved with insulin and the pancreas. Insulin 
is a vital hormone that acts like a key, allowing sugars in the bloodstream to move into the 
cells of the body. You’ve got to have insulin to live!
Type 2 diabetes is the most common form. It is a metabolic disorder — the pancreas is 
typically still producing insulin, but the body is resistant to it. Type 1 diabetes, on the 
other hand, is an autoimmune disease. The body destroys the insulin-producing cells 
in the pancreas, so the pancreas can no longer produce the hormone at all. This disease 
generally first shows up in children or teenagers. It is deadly.
Type 1 diabetics are dependent on frequent fingerprick blood tests, to check their blood 
sugar level, and insulin injections every time they eat, for the rest of their lives. Both high 
blood sugar (hyperglycemia) and low blood sugar (hypoglycemia) are dangerous. A 
diabetic’s life is a never-ending tightrope act between these two extremes, trying to 
maintain good blood sugar balance.
Treatment for Type 1 diabetes is advanced — as long as sufferers inject insulin before they 
eat, they can generally simply eat a healthy diet. They can enjoy a wide variety of foods, 
including sugar and other sweet things, as long as they accurately calculate how much 
carbohydrate they’re eating, and adjust their insulin.
Insulin pens make insulin injections relatively quick and easy. Insulin pumps, which allow 
easier and more accurate insulin delivery, without the need for daily needles, are another 
great advance.
Still, there are no holidays from this disease; a lapse can result in hospitalization, serious 
complications such as blindness, or worse.
Research programs around the world are trying to find out why Type 1 diabetes occurs, 
and why rates are rising. Diabetics haven’t lived unhealthy lives, they haven’t lived on 
sweets and sodas, and their parents haven’t done anything wrong. They just got sick, 
often out of the blue. There are endless efforts looking for a cure, and many exciting new 
possibilities. We can only hope that we’re living in the era when a cure will be found for 
this life-threatening disease.

83
