84
Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 
Puzzle 41: Obscure Vocabulary
Difficulty Level: Hard
Look out! I’ve included four extra words here that aren’t in the grid.
There are thousands of weird words out there — many are old-fashioned and have fallen 
out of use, but others are obscure terms or unusual jargon. Some of these words deserve 
to be slipped into your daily conversation — if nothing else, they can gain admiration 
from your friends and befuddle your enemies! One of my favorites is trottoir — a much 
cuter word for sidewalk originating from French. Here are few other gems to try out:
You can call that annoying kid (you know who I mean) a limaceous child, meaning you 
think he’s slug-like. He’s probably also otiose (idle and useless), tritical (trite), and a 
fabulist (liar).
If you’re very lucky, you may have a partner who’s keen on abliguration — extravagant 
cooking. This may lead to a nimiety (surplus) of food, though, so be prepared to become 
zaftig (desirably curvaceous and pleasingly plump). Whenever you come across marzi-
pan, make sure you call it marchpane.
I’m sure you know someone who is an ailurophile — he or she is abnormally fond of 
cats. Halieutics is the art of fishing, and to bombilate is to make a buzzing or droning 
sound.
These medical terms can come in handy, especially if you’re just a little ill, but want it 
to sound worse: Pyrexia is another term for fever, and cardialgia — although it sounds 
serious — is just heartburn or mild indigestion. The snake-entwined rod that symbolizes 
medicine is a caduceus.
Ataraxia sounds like some sort of medical condition, but it actually means absolute 
tranquility and freedom from all worries. Ergasiophobia is the fear of working or work 
in general. You can combine these terms quite effectively: “Please, sir, I need a lengthy 
vacation to treat my ergasiophobia and ataraxia.”
If you get hooked on unusual words, you can find many dictionaries on the Internet and 
in print. Just make sure you use your superior vocabulary powers for good! Gramercy 
(thank you)!

86
Part II: Starting with Straightforward Puzzles 

Chapter 5
Finding Hidden Words 
in Your Puzzles 
H
idden Words is another very common variety of word search. The 
words appear in the standard eight directions, but they’re more solidly 
packed and overlap more frequently.
As with most searches, you end up with a few unclaimed letters in the grid 
after you find all the list words. These leftover letters hold the key; they spell 
out a hidden message, usually when you look at them in normal reading order 
(left to right, row by row from the top to the bottom). The hidden message 
can be one or two words, or even a whole sentence in bigger puzzles. 
Figure 5-1 shows you an example of an Australian-themed word search; 
the leftover letters spell out Australia’s national capital.
 
Draw a circle around the leftover letters, or mark them in some other way 
(perhaps another colored highlighter). Go through the grid again carefully if 
the letters don’t spell anything — it’s easy to miss a letter here and there.
In harder Hidden Words puzzles, the leftover letters may be jumbled up, in 
which case you need to unscramble them to get the final answer. That’s just 
an added bit of complexity to annoy — I mean, challenge — you. I let you 
know if the hidden answer is jumbled in this chapter’s puzzles. Additionally, 
some puzzles have extra list words; I give you a heads up on this, too.

The left-
overs in 
this Hidden 
Words 
puzzle 
spell out 
Australia’s 
national 
capital.

Chapter 6
Solving Secret Shapes 
I
’m rather partial to these word searches (but then again, I invented them, 
so maybe I’m not the most objective judge). Secret Shapes are very simi-
lar to the Hidden Words puzzles, but they use a drawn grid so that each letter 
sits in a box.
After you complete the puzzle normally, you have leftover letters, but they 
don’t spell out any sort of message. Instead, you now color in the leftover 
letter squares (like in Figure 6-1) to discover a little picture! Another method 
for solving these puzzles is to lightly shade in the letter squares as you dis-
cover the words, and leave the shape uncolored.
The word list on each of these puzzles includes one word that gives the name 
of the Secret Shape so you know that you’ve got it right (or that you need to 
keep looking).
 
Figure 6-1: 
Coloring in 
the leftover 
letters 
reveals a 
shape!

Part III
Moving On to 
Complex Puzzles 

In this part . . .
H
ere are the challenging puzzles! In this part, you can 
find the Scrambled and Winder Words and Quiz and 
Secret List Word Searches, as well as a chapter of anagram 
puzzles. The puzzles in this part come in Easy, Medium, 
and Hard difficulty levels, so you can ease into each variety 
and hone your skills.
Don’t be put off just because these puzzles are “complex” — 
they’re really just simple puzzles with a little extra twist 
(literally, in the case of Winder Words). After all, variety is 
the spice of life. Just be sure to read any introductory text 
carefully for special instructions.

Chapter 7
Gathering Your Wits 
for Scrambled Searches
You notice the difference with this type of word search right away — the 
word list is gobbledygook! Your first challenge with one of these puzzles 
is to unscramble each word. Then you can get down to finding the words in 
the grid.
 
Each scrambled word contains the letters for the unscrambled word in its 
entirety (but nothing else). Each word is scrambled individually — the letters 
from other words on the list haven’t been mixed in too.
Here are some strategies for unscrambling words. You need some scrap 
paper and a pen or pencil:
 
✓ Write the letters in a circle, or write the scrambled word backward.
 
✓ Write the vowels in one group, and the consonants in another.
 
✓ Look for common letter patterns. See Chapter 14 for some helpful lists.
 
✓ If you like to play around with technology, and your brain hurts from 
these dratted muddled letters, you can find many online anagram solv-
ers. Simply type “solve anagrams online” into the search engine of your 
choice and take your pick! Here are a few to get you started:
 
• Crossword Dictionary Online: www.crossword-dictionary.
com/anagram.asp
 
• Internet Anagram Server: wordsmith.org/anagram/
 
• One Across: www.oneacross.com/anagrams/
 
• The Anagram Engine: www.easypeasy.com/anagrams/
 
• Zyzzyva (a free downloadable program): www.zyzzyva.net

Chapter 8
Are You Ready for Some 
Quiz Words?
Quiz Word Searches involve more mental effort than most word searches. 
They have letter grids, but no word lists. Instead they present you with 
a series of numbered quiz questions to answer, and the answers become your 
word list for the word search. These puzzles are a bit like a crossword in some 
ways — you have to answer the questions before you can complete the puzzle.
The Quiz Word Searches in this book range from small grids with easier quiz 
questions to large grids with downright difficult clues.
 
If you get stuck at any point, refer to the hints section in Chapter 12 — I give 
you the answer to at least one quiz question from each puzzle so you don’t 
have to head to the answers section right away.
These puzzles have two aspects: the quiz and the word search. I give you details 
about each in the following sections. But here’s the simplest explanation of these 
puzzles: All you need to do is answer the questions, and then find them in the 
grid. Each clue has the number of letters in parentheses after it, and the answers 
usually appear in alphabetical order (I tell you when this isn’t the case).
Taking the Quiz
You need your general knowledge to solve the quiz questions. You may need 
to rope in a spare brain (usually attached to a friend), or do some research 
for the harder puzzles in this section.
 
Feel free to use the Internet or reference books such as a dictionary, encyclo-
pedia, or atlas to search for answers. You’re not cheating in any way, I promise!
The puzzle may not have enough room for you to write your answers/word 
list, so you may need a sheet of scrap paper.

188
Part III: Moving On to Complex Puzzles 
 
A pencil and eraser are advisable for these more difficult puzzles, 
especially for writing out the quiz answers.
Turning to the Grid
When you have your answers, search for the words in the letter 
grid by using the standard word search methods covered in 
Chapter 2.
If you really can’t find one of your answers in the grid, you may 
need to consider the rather galling possibility that your answer is 
incorrect. Don’t take it hard; just reconsider the clue and try again. 
No one needs to know (and I certainly won’t tell anyone).
 
If you’re getting very stuck, try scanning the grid for words to see 
whether anything pops out at you. You can then match up any 
words you find with the quiz questions they (hopefully) answer. 
This method is perfectly valid and is just as challenging to the 
brain.
 
Be careful — word search grids hold many more accidentally cre-
ated words than those on the word list. Make sure that any words 
you discover this way really are the answers to your quiz questions!

Part III: Moving On to Complex Puzzles 

Chapter 9
Solving Secret List Word Searches
T
hese puzzles are probably the hardest in the book: I’ve hidden a whole 
list of words in a letter grid as usual, but I don’t give you the word list 
at all. How mean is that? To soften the blow, I do tell you the theme of each 
puzzle, how many words you need to find, and how long each word is. Easy 
and Medium puzzles have their word lists in alphabetical order and have hint 
letters scattered through.
Follow these steps to solve a Secret List Word Search:
 
1. Gather your information.
 
 Familiarize yourself with the number of words you need to find and what 
the topic is. If the puzzle gives you any other information (such as word 
lengths, hint letters, and so on), make a note of this as well.
 
2. Brainstorm possible examples that fit the theme.
 
 If the topic is one you’re familiar with, jot down the words you know 
off the top of your head. Think on the subject a bit longer — can you 
remember any other words? You may know of some others but not be 
sure of the spelling. Check your dictionary, a favorite book on the sub-
ject, or some Web sites if you need backup.
 
3. Check the letter grid for your brainstormed words.
 
 They’re probably there. You can also scan the grid to see whether any-
thing pops out that refreshes your memory. As long as the words you 
find relate to the theme, you can be pretty confident they’re the ones 
you’re looking for.
 
4. If you’re still stuck, walk away for a while.
 
 These puzzles are supposed to be fun! Don’t turn to the answer page 
right away. Move on to another puzzle if you’re getting very frustrated.
 
 If you leave your brain with a problem, it does its best to come up with 
the goods after some sleep. The effect is well-known, and one you can 
take advantage of. Go over the problem just before you go to sleep, 
and keep a notebook and pen next to your bed to catch that moment 
of inspiration!

Chapter 10
Weaving Your Way through 
Winder Words
T
his sort of word search puzzle differs from other kinds in a very funda-
mental way: Instead of running in straight lines, these words can twist 
through the letter grid turning at right angles (but not along diagonals). The 
letters can overlap, too.
The earliest word search puzzles were first published in the mid-1800s, and 
they were often similar to this style of puzzle. The compiler would generally 
fit a whole quotation into a square, along a winding path. Check out Chapter 1 
for an example of these early puzzles. These winding word puzzles were the 
forerunner of the standard word search puzzles you’re more familiar with. 
You can find the modern version of these puzzles in a variety of puzzle 
publications, so you may well be familiar with them under a different name.
 
Solving these devils requires slightly different searching methods than you 
need for other word searches. Instead of looking along a straight line after you 
find the first letter of a word, you need to keep checking around every letter in 
the word. However, you don’t have to do a full circle check because the words 
don’t turn on the diagonal; just check the letter above, below, and to either 
side in a cross or T check.
 
You may discover the occasional alternative answer path for some words — 
that’s perfectly fine. The answers in Chapter 13 are the pathways I designed 
for the puzzles, but if you find a different answer, that’s valid too. Most of 
these puzzles don’t have hidden meanings, so choosing a different path won’t 
create any problems.

Chapter 11
Discovering Anagrams 
and Other Word Games 
T
his chapter presents four different sorts of word puzzles to challenge 
your brain:
 
✓ Mix-Ups have a 3-x-3, 4-x-4, or extra hard 5-x-5 grid of random letters. 
Find words within the grid by moving from one adjacent letter to 
another without reusing the same square in any given word. You can 
move in any direction, including diagonally. Add a time limit to up the 
challenge for yourself!
 
✓ Muddled Words require you to rearrange any or all of the seven given 
letters into as many words as you can, using each letter only once.
 
✓ Daisy Words ask you to rearrange the letters on the daisy petals into as 
many words as possible without repeating letters. The catch: The letter 
on the shaded petal must appear in every word.
 
✓ Anagrams give you weird phrases that you must rearrange to form 
particular words according to the theme. Each anagram has a single 
answer; you don’t need to find a list of all possible words.
 
Words must be at least three letters long — no proper nouns allowed!
See Chapter 2 for tips and tricks to help you solve these and other anagram-
style puzzles. The letter pattern information in Chapter 14 is also very helpful 
when you solve these puzzles. In most of these puzzles, I’ve kept the answer 
list to words that are in general use  — some words in the Hard puzzles are 
unusual, but they’re not foreign or archaic terms.
 
For an added challenge, use only words in a particular dictionary (the 
Scrabble dictionary is a great example). And if you want to increase the 
number of possible words, add two-letter words to the game.

Part IV 
Hints and Answers

In this part . . .
I
’m revealing all — yes, I’m giving you the hints and 
answers! If you’re stuck on a puzzle, try the hints in 
Chapter 12 first. The kinds of hints vary — the starting 
point of a particular word in the grid, the number of 
words that start in a particular row or column of the grid, 
or the answer to a quiz question or other part of a com-
plex puzzle — but each and every puzzle has its own 
(hopefully) helpful tidbit. 
The answers are in Chapter 13, with every word shown in 
every grid, if you need them. I also give you the answers 
to the extra puzzle parts, such as extra words, hidden 
messages, quiz answers, unscrambled words, and so on.

Chapter 12
Helpful Hints to Keep You Going 
In This Chapter
▶ Using a coordinate system to navigate the grid
▶ Refusing to admit defeat (otherwise known as getting a hint)
E
ach puzzle in this book has a hint or two that you can easily access here 
without spoiling the rest of the puzzle by having to check the answer 
in Chapter 13. These hints vary in form: revealing a few words in the grid, 
answering quiz questions, unscrambling jumbled words, and so on.
Understanding the Grid 
Coordinate System
I use coordinates to indicate where a word starts in the letter grid. Think 
about the grid coordinate system you use to pinpoint and direct your deadly 
attacks (or, in my case, frequent misses) when playing Battleships. Here’s 
what you need to know about the coordinate system:
 
✓ The word in ALL CAPITALS is the word to search for in the letter grid.
 
✓ The first coordinate is the x coordinate and runs left to right along the 
top of the grid. In this book, I label each column on this axis with a capi-
tal letter, starting with A.
 
✓ The second coordinate is the y coordinate and runs from the top to 
bottom of the grid. Here I number each row consecutively, beginning 
with 1 (a good place to start, no?).
The grid in Figure 12-1 shows 12 columns, labeled A to L, and 9 numbered 
rows. You can find the word BASIL starting at position (A, 2) — the point 
where column A and row 2 intersect. So if you get a hint reading “PETAL 
starts at (H, 4),” you can expect to find a P at the intersection of column H 
and row 4. You can then check the eight squares around the P until you find 
the E, the T, and so on.

Proverbs within a Maze
A hard beginning maketh a good ending.
The road to a friend’s house is never long.
He who knows nothing, doubts nothing.
Praise the young and they will blossom.
The palest ink is better than the best memory.

Part IV: Hints and Answers 

Part V 
The Part of Tens

In this part . . .
T
here’s an unwritten — no, actually, it’s a written rule 
— that every For Dummies book has a Part of Tens, 
and this book is no different. In this part, you can discover 
the letter patterns that frequently occur in English, how 
solving word searches can benefit you, and even how to 
write your own word search puzzles!

Chapter 14 
Ten Letter-Pattern Tips
In This Chapter
▶ Discovering ten common features of English
▶ Finding out a little about letter frequency analysis
▶ Putting letter frequencies to use in puzzle solving
P
eople have been writing codes and ciphers for as long as writing (and 
hiding secret messages) has been around — thousands of years. The 
science of encoding and decoding codes and ciphers is called cryptography 
(or cryptology), from the Greek for “hidden writing.”
In this chapter, I introduce you to ten different aspects of English that can 
help in puzzle solving. Although you can certainly get by without knowing 
about letter patterns, this information can take your puzzle solving and word 
game skills to a new level.
Understanding cryptography
To better understand cryptography, you first 
need some very brief definitions:
 ✓ A cipher encodes a message by mixing up or 
replacing its letters. In a substitution cipher, 
each letter in the alphabet is encoded by 
one symbol. For example, the letter E may 
be encoded by an S, an 8, or a ♣.
 ✓ A code is effectively a language. Whole 
words are encoded, often by a single 
symbol, word, or signal. So the word cup-
cake may be encoded as 314, blue, or ♦ 
(because, you know, there are all those 
secret messages flying around out there 
about baked goods).
As you can see, cracking a code is much more 
difficult than solving a cipher — you’ve really 
got to steal the codebook to figure it out.

406
Part V: The Part of Tens 
Letters by Frequency
Letter frequency analysis is a statistical method that provides an effective 
way to crack substitution ciphers. When you use letter frequency analysis 
to crack a cipher, you simply count how many of each symbol appear in a 
section of encoded text. If it’s English, you can be fairly certain that the four 
most commonly occurring symbols are E, T, O, and A and that E is your most 
frequently seen letter. Then you look at things like commonly occurring pairs 
of letters (digraphs) and short words. The process is very involved if you’re a 
professional cryptographer, of course, but you don’t need to worry about the 
complicated version for your puzzle solving needs.
If you can remember even some of this information, you can bring it into play 
when searching for words within word search grids, solving anagrams, and 
looking for words in word games.
No one can compile the definitive list of the frequency of letters in English 
(or any language) because this list changes depending on which texts a 
person has pulled apart and analyzed. I’ve chosen one list for you, to keep 
things less confusing, but if you’re interested in finding out more, you can 
discover more online or in a book on cryptography.
You read the list from left to right, from most frequently used letters to the 
least. I’ve broken it into five-letter groups (and six at the end) just to make 
it a bit easier for you to read. So here it is — the frequency of letters in 
English:
ETAON RISHD LFCMU GYPWB VKXJQZ
This information is crucial when you solve cryptogram puzzles, crosswords 
with no clues, and other ciphers. It is also helpful when you’re looking for 
solutions to anagrams and playing word-based games like Scrabble, Boggle, 
and Hangman.
You can also use letter frequency knowledge when solving word searches. 
A good strategy is to search for the less frequently used letters within a word 
rather than a more common initial letter. For example, search for NIRVANA 
in a letter grid by looking for the rare V rather than the more common initial 
letter N.
You can also expect that words consisting of the most common letters, such 
as TENANT, NEATEN, ANTENNA, and ENTREAT, will be harder to find in 
letter grids because these letter occur so frequently in the grid.

407
 Chapter 14: Ten Letter-Pattern Tips 
Common First Letters
The 12 most common starting letters in English text (in descending order) 
are TAI SOW CMB PHD. This order isn’t a reflection of how many words in the 
dictionary start with T — S is the leader there. Being the most common start-
ing letter means that more words start with T than with other letters in any 
given piece of English writing. A letter’s popularity has to do with repeated 
usage; to, that, this, the, there, and so on are very popular words!
This information about first letters is very handy when you’re playing games 
such as Boggle or Scrabble, looking for words in a Secret List Word Search, 
or cracking a cryptogram. When you’re searching for new words in anagram-
type puzzles and games (such as the ones in Chapter 11), look through the 
letters in play — on tiles, on the board, or written down — for these common 
starting letters. You can also test the encoded words in cryptograms against 
this list.
Eh-tay-oh-in shird-loo and other tales 
of letter frequency
ETAOIN SHRDLU is a famous frequency distri-
bution of the most popular 12 letters in English, 
associated with the first Linotype machines 
(and with the mnemonic device in this sidebar’s 
title), but it’s not the only ranking uncovered by 
people who probably had far too much time on 
their hands.
The Brown Corpus (one million words carefully 
chosen in the 1960s) gives the top ten letters in 
American English as ETAOI NSRHL. If you break 
down the literary classics, you get results such as 
ETAOI NHSRD (Pride and Prejudice) and ETAOI 
HNSRD (Alice in Wonderland) — very similar!
In more recent times, others have sampled 
several gigabytes of Usenet traffic (ETAOIN 
SRHLDC), or the word list (including hyphenated 
words and phrases) from Webster’s Unabridged 
Dictionary (EAIRON TSLCUD). Another popular 
listing (and the one I’ve always remembered) is 
ETAON RISHD — it’s the one I use in this book.
Different writers and subjects yield different 
letter frequencies. These discrepancies can 
even help figure out whether someone is really 
the author of a piece or not!
Different languages have a different spread of 
letters, as you may expect, so if you’re practic-
ing your French vocabulary in Hangman, the top 
10 letters to focus on are ESAIT NRULO.
According to various analyses, German’s letter 
distribution comes in as ENRIS TUDAH and 
Italian has AEIOL NRTSC; all languages have 
different letter frequencies.
As you can see this topic gets incredibly com-
plicated very rapidly! E is clearly English’s most 
common letter, but the finer details of letter fre-
quencies are the realm of the cryptologist.

408
Part V: The Part of Tens 
Common Last Letters
The 12 most common ending letters in English are EST NDR YOL AFG.
If you’re very good at anagram-based games, you can work backward from a 
last letter to the start of the word! This information is also helpful when solv-
ing cryptograms.
When solving anagrams and scrambled word puzzles, try putting some of 
these letters (especially E, S, and T) in the end positions, to see if that helps 
solve the puzzles. For cryptograms, look for patterns in the encoded symbols 
that appear as final letters, and see if these common ending letters fit the bill.
Follow Me: Common Letter Sequences
If you can remember which letters are most likely to follow others, you’ll be 
faster at playing games like Boggle and Scrabble and cracking anagrams and 
ciphers. Knowing which letter patterns to follow in a grid and which ones you 
can safely ignore can also speed up your word search play. Keep the following 
patterns in mind:
 
✓ Q is always followed by U — well, almost always; a few Arabic and Chinese 
words such as qanat and qigong are slowly entering the English language.
 
✓ If you have a vowel, the consonant that most often follows it is N; AN, 
EN, IN, ON, UN — they’re all popular!
 
✓ E is most likely to be followed by R, S, N, D, or another E.
 
✓ T is most likely to be followed by H, E, I, O, or another T.
 
✓ O is most likely to be followed by N, F, R, or another O.
 
✓ N is most likely to be followed by D or another N.
 
✓ R is most likely to be followed by E or another R.
 
✓ I is most likely to be followed by N.
 
✓ S is most likely to be followed by T or S.
 
✓ H is most likely to be followed by E or I.
Two’s Company: Digraphs
Pairs of letters that form one sound are called digraphs (or bigrams). If you 
can remember the more common ones, they can give you a boost when 
cracking ciphers, solving anagrams, and playing many word games, from 
word searches and crosswords to board games.

409
 Chapter 14: Ten Letter-Pattern Tips 
Here is a list of the more common ones (roughly) by frequency:
TH, HE, AN, RE, ER, IN, ON, AT, ND, ST, ES, EN, OF, TE, ED, OR, TI, AS, 
and TO
Other digraphs to keep in mind are
AE, AU, BL, BR, BY, CH, CK, CL, CR, DL, DR, EU, FR, FL, GH, GL, KN, LM, 
MP, NC, NG, NS, OU, PH, PR, PY, RD, RF, RV, SH, SW, TW, US, VR, WN, WR, 
ZY, and a whole lot more!
Make That Three: Trigraphs
Trigraphs, or sets of three letters that make one sound, are common in English 
words. Knowing some of these trigraphs (also known as trigrams) can help you 
find better anagrams, tricky words in Boggle, and great moves in Scrabble!
The following are the top ten most frequent trigraphs:
THE, ING, AND, ION, ENT, FOR, TIO, ERE, HER, and ATE
A few (of many more) trigraphs to remember:
ANT, ART, AST, EDT, NCE, NCY, NDE, NGL, OFT, SCH, SCR, SHR, SPR, 
SQU, STH, THA, THR, TIS, and TRY
Double the Fun: Double Letters
Double letters are a great way into word searches — they’re usually easy to spot 
in the grid. They’re also very helpful when solving cryptograms and anagrams!
The most commonly occurring double letters in English, from most to least 
common, are LL, EE, SS, OO, TT, FF, RR, NN, PP, and CC.
Common Prefixes
These prefixes are handy to remember as good word-starters when playing 
any anagram game. They’re very useful for extending words, so they’re 
perfect when you’re playing games like Boggle, Upwords, and Scrabble. 
Here’s a list of some common English prefixes:

410
Part V: The Part of Tens 
UN, RE, IN, IM, IR, IL, DIS, EN, EM, NON, OVER, MIS, SUB, PRE, INTER, DE, 
FORE, TRANS, BE, ANTI, SUPER, SEMI, ANTI, MID, UNDER, CO, OUT, AN, 
AB, AD, TRI, BI, NEO, COM, EX, BE, ANTE, CONTRA, DIA, EXTRA, HEMI, 
HYPER, HYPO, PERI, POST, ULTRA, and SYN
Common Suffixes
Use suffixes to extend words, especially in games like Boggle, Upwords, and 
Scrabble. If you have a base word such as hand, you can make handful, hand-
ier, and handy. Some common English suffixes include the following:
TION, AL, ENT, IVE, LY, ITY, ER, OUS, NESS, DOM, IZE, FUL, FY, EN, IC, 
MENT, ISM, ATE, SION, ABLE, Y, ANCE, SHIP, ENCE, AGE, LESS, ERY, OR, 
EST, IST, ACY, and BLE
Common Consonant Patterns 
at the Beginnings of Words
These vowel-free digraphs often appear at the beginnings of words. They can 
help you find words in Secret List Word Searches, solve cryptograms, and 
beat your opponents in various word games. Common consonant combos 
(say that three times fast) include ST, SH, STR, SP, TR, TH, PR, CH, and CHR.
Everyday cryptography
You may think that the information in this chap-
ter doesn’t have much impact on you, apart from 
improving your puzzling skills. However, millions 
of people rely on high quality encryption every 
day, not just secret agents and governments. 
Cryptography is a thriving field of research and 
development.
Every time you make an online payment, very 
complicated and advanced digital encryption is 
used to ensure your transaction is secure. The 
SSL (Secure Sockets Layer) protocol is currently 
widely used. If the Web site has https as the 
prefix rather than http, you’re protected by 
SSL security. Also look for the little lock symbol 
in the corner of your browser’s menu bar. Make 
sure it’s closed (not open) before sending any 
personal or credit card details!
And while I’m talking about online security, 
please, never ever send your personal, credit 
card, or online account details to anyone (not 
even your mom) in an e-mail. Sending an e-mail 
is like sending a postcard; the mailman, the 
government, or someone unscrupulous (or all 
three) can read it along the way.

Chapter 15
Ten Benefits of Doing 
Word Searches 
In This Chapter
▶ Improving your word power
▶ Having fun and relaxing
S
olving word searches has some real benefits, so I advocate carrying a 
book of puzzles with you at all times! This chapter shows you why this 
recommendation isn’t as crazy as it sounds.
They’re Portable Boredom-Busters
Word searches are one of the best portable puzzles. You don’t need a diction-
ary, a thesaurus, or online resources, and you can probably even get away 
without needing an eraser! Pop a book of word searches and a pen into your 
bag or briefcase. Any time you’re kicking your heels or the kids are acting up, 
you’re ready to entertain yourself or them.
They Keep Your Brain Active
A lot of evidence suggests that staying mentally active helps to stave off 
dementia. If nothing else, doing word searches helps you stay interested in 
and involved with the world around you. See Chapter 1 for more information.
They Increase Your Vocabulary
Vocabulary-building is one of the biggest benefits of solving word searches. 
Didn’t know urad was a type of bean until you saw it in a bean-themed word 

412
Part V: The Part of Tens 
search? Now you do! Word searches are also great if you want to become 
more familiar with the jargon of a new field. Make a list of words you want 
to add to your repertoire and use them to create your own word search. 
Chapter 16 tells you how to write your own puzzles.
They Help You Learn New Languages
If you’re learning a second, third, or seventh language, word searches are a 
great way to practice your spelling and add to the lists of words you know. 
Try making a word search puzzle (using the directions in Chapter 16) of your 
weekly vocabulary goals.
Word searches are also a very valuable tool for the brave souls faced with 
the almost impossible task of learning to speak and read English as a second 
language. It is one of the most confusing languages of all time with so many 
spelling variants, unusual pronunciations, and irregular forms.
They Help You Gather Knowledge in 
a Way that Makes Sense to You
A popular educational theory (sometimes called multiple intelligences) states 
that different people learn in different ways. This theory outlines eight differ-
ent types of learners; three of these learning types are particularly well suited 
to word searches:
 
✓ Linguistic or verbal learners are word smart — they learn best by reading 
and listening.
 
✓ Visual-spatial learners are picture smart, thinking in pictures.
 
✓ Logical-mathematical learners are number and reasoning smart and are 
very good at pattern-seeking and logic.
Word searches are particularly well suited to these learning styles, because 
they focus on words, letter shapes, patterns, and systematic grid-scanning.
They Let You Relax and Have Fun
No doubt about it — having fun is good for you! If the troubles of life are 
becoming too much, break out the puzzle book and take your mind some-
where else for a few minutes. Word searches are great for this because 
they’re easier than other word puzzles. They aren’t too demanding if you’re 

413
 Chapter 15: Ten Benefits of Doing Word Searches
not up to something hard, but they’re still a mental challenge. If you’re in the 
10 percent of people who struggle with dyslexia or another literacy challenge, 
this nonthreatening test is especially nice. Even if you’re a bad speller, you 
don’t have to give up word searches.
Life has many problems to solve, both large and small, from what to cook 
for dinner to how to live more lightly on Earth — and they don’t always have 
clear-cut answers. Solving something, even just a word search, and being con-
fident that you can solve it is satisfying.
They Sharpen Your Spelling Skills
Word searches are a great way to improve your spelling. You need to know 
how to spell to do a crossword, but a word search can actually help you learn 
to spell better by actively looking for the set of letters in order.
Looking at the search words in close detail in the list and then searching 
for them letter by letter in the puzzle helps to cement their spelling in your 
brain. You don’t do this kind of deconstruction when reading normally, and it 
really helps in remembering details of spelling. Word searches don’t feel like 
work, so they’re especially good for encouraging children to practice spelling 
without feeling like it’s hard work.
They Train Your Brain 
to Recognize Patterns
Pattern recognition — the ability to identify letters, faces, and all manner of 
objects — is a basic cognitive skill (the others include such useful gems as 
decision-making, focus and concentration, memory, problem solving, and cat-
egorization). It’s the process of matching your memory to what you’re seeing 
and is a vital skill in reading, among many other things.
Humans can recognize any letter, whether it’s a different size, strange type-
face, color, handwritten, or drawn at an angle, which is pretty remarkable 
when you stop to think about it. In Figure 15-1, you probably recognize each 
of the characters as being the letter K even though they all look different.
Although these recognition processes happen quickly, achieving accurate 
recognition without having to even think about it takes a huge amount of 
practice. Solving word search puzzles can be a fun part of this practicing. 
The benefit is in having to focus on finding a particular shape (say, an M) 
within the confusing grid of shapes (the letters, that is). Scanning up and 
down the columns of letters searching for that particular letter pattern 

414
Part V: The Part of Tens 
helps to train the brain to recognize the shape, name it, and then key into 
it. This practice is great for those new to the English alphabet, as well as for 
people recovering from strokes or brain injuries.
 
Figure 15-1: 
Pattern 
recognition 
tells you 
these 
letters are 
Ks despite 
their visual 
differences.
 
They Help You Overcome Insomnia
You know what it’s like. You can’t drop off to sleep in the first place, and then 
when you finally do, you wake up in the middle of the night. Your brain keeps 
going over and over all those problems, which seem so much worse at 3 a.m. 
In these dire circumstances, reach for your word search puzzles.
Solve a simple word search puzzle or two at bedtime, or in the middle of the 
night when insomnia is plaguing you. This gentle mental activity pulls your 
thoughts away from those worries. It doesn’t matter if you drop off to sleep 
in the middle of the puzzle. There’s no plot or exciting story to be drawn 
into, and no complex concepts to come to grips with. The puzzle distracts 
your mind, helps you relax, and slip into longed-for sleep. Keep a volume of 
puzzles and a pencil by your bed — no doctor’s prescription required.
They’re a Way to Spend Quality 
Time with the Family
Word searches are one of the few puzzles that kids and adults can easily do 
together on an equal footing. So when the grandkids are visiting, or you’re all 
stuck in a tent while the rain keeps pouring, get out a book of word searches 
(oh look, you’ve got one in your hands already!), and settle down to solve the 
puzzles together. It’s always nice to be able to do something so satisfying and 
simple together!

Chapter 16 
Ten Steps to Help You Design 
Your Own Word Search
In This Chapter
▶ Picking up puzzle writing skills
▶ Improving your vocabulary and spelling effortlessly
D
o you want an even bigger mental challenge than just solving a puzzle? 
How about writing one of your own? A word search is a relatively easy 
puzzle to write, and a good place to start if you’re new to this lark. A small 
word search can take as little as an hour to create.
 
You can find free word search generators online, and you can purchase inex-
pensive software programs that create word searches for you. Although these 
programs create the puzzles quickly, the puzzles are often predictable, medio-
cre, and poorly designed. And, face it, you aren’t getting a mental workout by 
plugging words into the software program.
You can come up with a better puzzle — and exercise your brain — by creating 
one yourself. Writing a word search is also a very good educational exercise 
for kids, but don’t tell them that! Using the steps outlined here, you may be sur-
prised at how quickly you can create a puzzle of your very own. And you may 
find doing something without a computer for a change to be very satisfying.
Step 1: Choose a Theme
Your first task is to decide what purpose and/or theme you want for your 
puzzle. You don’t have to have a reason or a theme, of course, but it does 
make the word selection more interesting. Some ideas:
 
✓ Helping your kids learn their spelling lists or supplementing a home-
schooling project
 
✓ Practicing vocabulary for a new language

416
Part V: The Part of Tens 
 
✓ Creating personalized gifts or fun cards for friends and family
 
✓ Exploring topical subjects, local politics, or history
 
✓ Commemorating holidays and other celebrations
Step 2: Gather Your Words
I admit it, this step is my favorite part of writing word searches, but then I am 
a bit weird. You have to keep only a couple of points in mind here:
 
✓ Gather a lot more words than you think you need — at least twice as 
many. A 13 x 13 word search generally has about 15 to 20 words in it, so 
collect 30 to 40 words for a puzzle this size, if possible.
 
✓ Avoid three-letter words as much as possible. Aim to have your short-
est words four letters long. Lots of three-letter words are accidentally 
created in any letter grid, and you’ll end up with unintended duplicates 
if you use many short words.
 
 If some high quality words (important on-topic words) are three letters 
long, include them by all means, but make sure you check the grid care-
fully to ensure these short words haven’t been accidentally duplicated. 
The later section “Check and Recheck the Puzzle” has more detail on 
how to search for duplicates.
Let’s say you’re writing a word search for your dad’s birthday. Make a long 
list of any words relating to his family, career, and life: favorite hobbies, 
sporting teams, music, books, foods and drinks, pets, and so on.
Include variations on key words, such as shortened versions of names and 
nicknames (JENNIFER, JENNY, JEN, JAY RAY) and different forms of nouns and 
verbs (SONG, SONGS, SING, SINGS, SINGING, SANG, SINGER, SINGERS). Also use 
made-up family words if you’re going for the silly option. (I thought everyone 
said “killerpillers,” but apparently it’s actually “caterpillars.” Who knew?)
Write the master word list as you go, either handwriting the words on a piece 
of graph paper or typing them in a monospaced typeface such as Lucida 
Console or Monaco (head to “Make It Pretty” later in this chapter to find out 
more about monospaced typefaces).
 
Make sure the words in your master list are all lined up so you can easily see 
the letter patterns. This trick helps you quickly see which letters come first, 
second, third, and so on in each word and how long each word is. It makes 
your task of filling in the letter grid much easier. This strategy highlights words 

417
 Chapter 16: Ten Steps to Help You Design Your Own Word Search 
good for overlapping. You can quickly scan your word list for a particular 
pattern, such as “something, something, A, something, something, T,” or to 
find any word that has L as the third letter. Figure 16-1 gives you an example — 
you can clearly see the third letters of each word by reading down the columns.
 
Figure 16-1: 
Write 
out your 
working 
word list 
in a grid 
or mono-
spaced 
typeface.
 
Step 3: Make the Big Grid Decisions
Decide on your grid size and rough difficulty level. You can make your grid 
any size, really, but a very small grid is harder to fill because you don’t have 
as much wiggle room to fit all your words in. I recommend trying a 13 x 13 
grid for starters, and then creating smaller and larger grids as you get more 
experienced.
An easier word search (for the solver) has a smaller grid, shorter word list, 
and not so many backward or diagonal words. If you’re writing the puzzle for 
children, adjust the word list to be age-appropriate.
Step 4: Assemble the Tools of the Trade
Word searches are quite straightforward to write as well as solve. Your 
equipment list is small:
 
✓ Graph paper (quarter inch grid is a good choice)
 
✓ Nice paper (for your finished copy)
 
✓ Ruler
 
✓ Pen, pencil, and eraser
 
✓ Highlighter (optional)

418
Part V: The Part of Tens 
You may find it helpful to have an outside resource for finding words that fit 
into your puzzle. You can search for extra words by using online tools; my 
favorite is OneLook Dictionary Search (www.onelook.com). You can search 
for letter patterns to fit your grid and specify a theme, which is extremely 
helpful.
Step 5: Just Add Words
Now the fun starts — time to put those words into the grid! Place your longest 
and highest-quality words first. Progress from long words to shorter words as 
you put the puzzle together. Place diagonal words and words in the puzzle 
corners early on and gradually fill in the spaces around them. Bunches of 
words that run in the same direction are easiest to place. It’s harder to put a 
mix of word directions within the grid.
Scan through your master word list (see Figure 16-2 for an example) to find 
letter patterns that can overlap in the grid. An easy way to get some overlap 
is to have a word start on top of a letter within another word. Try to avoid 
crossing over letters in the grid more than twice in an easy-level puzzle. If 
you’re writing a really tricky puzzle, overlapping a letter three times is okay.
 
Figure 16-2: 
Scan this 
sample 
master 
word list 
before you 
start adding 
words to 
the grid.
 
 
Make a special note of the three- and four-letter words you place. You can mark 
the positions of the placed words in highlighter if you like. This strategy can 
help you keep track of which words you’ve placed and where they are (trust 
me, it gets confusing pretty quickly). The downside of this technique is that 
any marks you make aren’t easy to erase if you remove a word from the grid.

419
 Chapter 16: Ten Steps to Help You Design Your Own Word Search 
If you need to fill a position but don’t have a word from your master word 
list that fits, check out an online resource such as www.onelook.com. If one 
pattern doesn’t yield a good word, delete one letter at a time from the search 
parameters until you do get a good match (for instance, start with _ _ C_ _ _ T_, 
and then go to _ _C_ _ _T, and then _ _C_ _ _, and so on).
 
If you add extra words to your puzzle from such sources, make sure you add 
them to your master word list so the final word list is accurate. You don’t 
want to have words in the letter grid that aren’t in your word list, or — even 
worse — words in the word list that aren’t in the letter grid!
Figures 16-3 and 16-4 show an example of placing words in an Italian Foods 
word search. Start with PANCETTA — it’s a long word, so place it on the 
diagonal.
Time for the second word. ANTIPASTI, a nine-letter word that has T as the 
third letter ( _ _T _ _ _ _ _ _ ), fills in nicely.

You’ve got plenty of room, so try running the third word on a diagonal. You 
don’t need to overlap any letters, so it’s easy to place, but you want it to be 
at least five letters long. GNOCCHI works well, so in it goes!
You still have room to run another diagonal word alongside GNOCCHI, 
coming up from the other direction. It needs to start at position (H, 8), (G, 7), 
or (F, 6). (The coordinates pinpoint squares in the grid — the letter refers to 
the column and the number refers to the row. See Chapter 12 for more infor-
mation on the coordinate system.)
The word will overlap the C in PANCETTA; it can overlap the final I in 
ANTIPASTI, but it doesn’t have to. I’ve marked the position in Figure 16-4

So you need a word from your master list of Italian foods that can fit some-
where within the pattern _ _ _ _ C _ _ I.
Several options in the master word list (including TROCCOLI and ARANCIA) 
work here, but I like the sound of some snail-shaped pasta, so that’s what 
you’re picking (not to be a tyrant or anything). LUMACHE fits the bill nicely — 
this word overlaps the C but doesn’t use the I.
 
If you can’t find a word to fit in a space from one direction, see whether 
you can get better results by trying from the opposite direction. You’ll 
have more luck finding a word for the pattern _V _ _ E than E _ _ V _ , for 
instance.
 
Don’t duplicate words, or have short words that are contained within longer 
words in your word list — so don’t put the word CART in the grid as well as 
CARTOON.
You’re going well if you can fit 15 to 20 words into a 13 x 13 grid (169 squares). 
A 17 x 17 grid (289 squares) can easily hold 25 to 30 words.
Step 6: Ponder the Punctuation
Don’t include punctuation, spaces, or accents in the letter grid. The only excep-
tion to this rule is if you were writing a word search in a foreign language that 
uses additional characters. For example, a Danish word search would include 
the additional characters Æ, Ø, and Å.

421
 Chapter 16: Ten Steps to Help You Design Your Own Word Search 
However, include any punctuation, spaces, and accents in your word list. For 
example, if you were doing a word search of Beatles song titles, you would 
put BABYYOUREARICHMAN in the letter grid, but you’d write it as BABY 
YOU’RE A RICH MAN in the word list.
Step 7: Erase and Change as Needed
Be prepared to remove words, and change the grid if things don’t fit as well 
as you want. The eraser is your friend!
 
If you remove a word from the grid, be sure to check for words it overlapped. 
Don’t remove the overlapping letters, or you’ll screw up the other word 
as well. Also, reinstate the deleted word on your master word list to make it 
available again.
Sometimes you have words that you really want to include in the puzzle but 
that screw up the pattern and prevent other words from going in smoothly. 
Either be brave and restart the whole puzzle, putting these cherished words 
in first, or remove the words from the puzzle altogether.
When you’re happy with the placement of your words, write over them in ink 
(or make a copy of the puzzle in ink). In the master word list, highlight or 
otherwise mark the words you used.
Step 8: Fill in the Grid
After you get your words into the letter grid, it probably has some gaps. 
That’s when you complete your grid with fill, the mostly random letters that 
complete the puzzle.
To add random letters, refer to the letter pattern lists in Chapter 14 for the 
most common letters in English or just repeatedly write the alphabet ran-
domly into the blank squares until you fill them all in.
 
If you want to make the puzzle harder, avoid adding fill letters that you haven’t 
used in the word list. In other words (no pun intended), if you don’t have any 
words containing a Q in the puzzle grid, don’t put a Q in the fill. Generally, this 
strategy means you just have to keep tabs on J, V, X, Q, and Z.

422
Part V: The Part of Tens 
Step 9: Check and Recheck the Puzzle
Check the grid for duplicates and rude words. I’m not going to sully these lily-
white (okay, beige-ish) pages with the list of common swear words and other 
offensive terms, but you know the ones I mean.
 
Search through your finished word search letter grid for any three- or four-
letter words you want to avoid and for duplicates of the short words from 
your word list. Scan the grid twice, row by row and then column by column.
Your three-letter words are the biggest repetition hazard, followed by the 
four-letter words. Although you can get duplicates of longer words (or find 
longer rude words), that scenario is very unlikely.
 
If you do come across a problem — a duplicate, or a (gasp) rude word — you 
can nearly always correct the grid by changing just one or two of your random 
fill letters.
Step 10: Make It Pretty
After you compile and test your puzzle, you’re ready to prepare the artwork! 
You can create your final puzzle in one of two ways: using a computer or 
drawing it up by hand.
The following sections give you information on both of these methods. The 
main thing to be aware of with either technique is making sure that the let-
ters all line up.
Using a computer
Here’s how to draw the puzzle in a word processing program, such as 
Microsoft Word:
Open a new document and transcribe your word search into the file row by 
row. Use your ruler as a guide to read along your handwritten copy.
To get a good grid layout, you need a bit of space around the letters. One 
space between each letter and 1.5 line spacing can work well. You may want 
to muck around with tab settings, too.

423
 Chapter 16: Ten Steps to Help You Design Your Own Word Search 
Monospaced typefaces make the layout job much easier. Monospaced type-
faces, also called fixed-width or nonproportional typefaces, fit every letter into 
the same amount of space. So an I takes up the same room as an M — which 
isn’t the case in other fonts! The two grids in Figure 16-5 use two different 
monospaced typefaces (Geneva and Andale Mono).
 
Figure 16-5:

helps with

Andale Mono, Courier, Consolas, Lucida Console, Monaco, and Prestige Elite 
are all widely available monospaced typefaces, which you should be able to 
find on your Mac or PC.
 
The letters in the grid must all line up, both by rows and by column, and be 
roughly equidistant from each other.
Using all capital letters for list words is the convention, although a lower-case 
puzzle may be good for kids learning to recognize the alphabet.
Writing it out by hand
If you prefer to handwrite your finished puzzle, here’s how to go about it:
 
1. Draw up a grid the size of your finished puzzle, with one vertical line 
per column of letters, and one horizontal line per row of letters.
 
 You can go about this process in two ways:
 
A. Make your grid in ink or create it in a drawing program on your 
computer, and print it out. The inked grid goes under the piece 
of paper you’re working on (lightly tape the two pieces of paper 
together and lean up against a sunny window).
 
B. Draw your grid in light pencil lines on your paper. You can erase 
the lines later.

424
Part V: The Part of Tens 
 
2. Using your best and clearest handwriting, write the letter grid out, 
line by line, with each letter sitting directly on the intersecting lines.
 
 Figure 16-6 shows an example. This method ensures that the letters 
all line up properly. You may want to use calligraphy pens or a good 
black pen.
 
Figure 16-6: 
When draw-
ing a grid by 
hand, use a 
pencil grid 
to guide 
placement.
 
 
3. Erase the pencil lines when the ink is thoroughly dry.
 
 I mean it — leave it to dry for at least half an hour, even if you’ve used 
a felt-tipped pen. Trust me; I’ve made enough smudgy mistakes for the 
both of us.
 
4. Write out the words in the word list in alphabetical order next to or 
underneath the puzzle.
 
 Keep the case the same — if you’ve written the puzzle in all capital let-
ters, write the list in all capitals as well.
 
5. Decorate the page however you like, and then enjoy the delight of 
your recipient when he or she receives your hand-crafted puzzle!
That’s all, folks! The end! Go forth and puzzle!

Denise Sutherland is a syndicated Australian puzzle author. Her 
crosswords, word searches, and other puzzles appear in a wide range 
of Australian and international publications, and she has written five 
themed puzzle books. Sutherland runs her own graphic design business, 
Sutherland Studios, where she concentrates on creating handcrafted 
puzzles.

Become a word search 
whiz — and boost your 
brain power to boot!
Are you wild about word searches? This fun, go-anywhere 
guide is packed with puzzles to stretch your skills and 
strengthen your mind. Inside you’ll find eight different 
kinds of word searches — including one invented by the 
author! — as well as extensive tips and tricks to improve 
your puzzle-solving abilities. You’ll have a great time 
mastering increasingly difficult searches while you reap the 
brain-boosting benefits.
•  Working with word searches — get familiar with the eight word 
search varieties in the book and the strategies for solving each 
collection
•  Start with straightforward puzzles — try simple and story word 
searches, find hidden words, and solve secret-shape puzzles 
•  Conquer more complex searches — gather your wits for 
scrambles, quiz questions, and secret-list and winding word 
searches
•  Play like a pro with bonus puzzles — tackle anagrams and other 
word games that are guaranteed to make your brain hurt!
•  Try not to peek — the answers to all puzzles feature full grids, so 
you can see where each word is placed
